030758836X|5.0|This is an intense and scary (in many ways) look inside a marriage. Guy named Nick Dunne is married to gal named Amy Elliott. On the day of their 5-year anniversary, Nick is at work and receives a call from one of his neighbours \\"Nicky, I just thought you should know...your door is wide open, and that cat of yours is outside. It isn't supposed to be, right?\\"<br /><br />No it's not, so Nick goes home; Amy is not there.<br /><br />From being in Nick's head, the reader already knows that this marriage wasn't doing so well, mostly because Nick doesn't sound happy --and maybe a bit weird too, especially when he says \\"There's something disturbing about recalling a warm memory and feeling utterly cold.\\" Flynn switches her storytelling between both Nick in real time, and Amy from her diary entries; these entries start at the beginning of their relationship and follow their love story.<br /><br />The contrast between Nick's present unhappy marriage and Amy's storytelling of falling in love, and of their perfect match gives a stronger sense that something is really awry. Flynn exploits this brilliantly by throwing in unexpected sentences here and there that unbalances the reader, creating strong discomfort. While Nick is questioned by two detectives, he thinks \\"It was my fifth lie to the police. I was just starting.\\" And of course, the reader immediately thinks \\"well, what were the first four?\\" Later during the questioning by the detectives, Nick says to himself \\"This was my eleventh lie.\\"<br /><br />Gillian Flynn expertly manipulates the readers by having them constantly change their opinion about Nick depending on what he says, thinks, does, or how he acts, reacts, or stays passive. I've not often read a book where I've felt so absorbed and uncomfortable at the same time. The underlying creepiness and unsettling atmosphere coming off the page are as intense and as close as possible to experiencing it in real life.<br /><br />The marriage has gone insanely wrong. The readerrealizes that Nick and Amy are either very wrong for each other, or perfectly matched; you'll have to decide. But I warn you, there are no wrong answers; I'm tempted to say no right ones either. Gillian Flynn unspools a clever and intricate plot that is both machiavellian and pure genius in its depiction of a modern-day relationship. Being careful not to spoil anything to those who haven't read the book yet, I'll say this: when you've finished reading GONE GIRL, Michael Douglas and Glenn Close's relationship in \\"Fatal Attraction\\" will seem like a great love story to you.<br /><br />While all clues point towards Nick, and as his lies become apparent to the police, he becomes the main suspect in the disappearance of his wife. His nonchalant, almost bored attitude seem very suspicious to both the media and the public opinion too, especially since \\"the husband is almost always guilty\\". To deflect some of that tension, Gillian Flynn takes us deep into the couple's married life to show Nick and Amy at their best and worst. We learn about the loss of their jobs, about Amy's unhappiness regarding their move from New York City to a rented McMansion on the Mississippi, about the money problems, the question of having kids, and of secrets from before they knew each other.<br /><br />Gillian Flynn's sense of timing is as perfect as if she is building an enormous clock --or a time-machine-- that she uncovers one or two pieces at a time, while revealing Nick and Amy's day to day story. Flynn brings new developments exactly when you don't expect them, winding the story back in the opposite direction of where you thought it was heading, while the plot still keeps going forward. Flynn does that often but you follow willingly because it is always believable --you can still hear the tick-tock of the clock in the background, and the grinding of the time-machine-- and her moves are backed-up by elements she has spread throughout the pages (almost nonchalantly, you think) and everythingclicks together until you finally see the whole work. Or maybe not.<br /><br />Another aspect that proves Flynn's total control of the story is her ambidextrous talent for both male and female points of view, especially with the characters inner-dialogues which are a major part of the novel. She is as credible inside Nick's head as she is inside Amy's; not only that, but their changing moods and their actions are rendered as convincingly as any great actors could play them. Speaking of which, these two characters are dream roles for any talented actors who play within a wide range of emotions.<br /><br />And of course, GONE GIRL would be a terrific movie in the right director's hands. But read the book first, it is much better than any movie adaptation could be.

B002ZB26AO|5.0|Many books about North Korea that I have read display the absurdity, pain, sadness, and blight existence of a nation gone rogue and gone wrong.  Its blatantly obvious through these texts, histories, and other news outlets that North Korea is one of the most odd nations in surviving in a post-Communist world for so long at the cost of a miserable livelihood brought together by mismanagement, extreme repression, and an Orwellian control system.  Testimonies, documentaries, and research, as well as visits to the low-key state bring about the same motifs of blind acceptance of their dear leaders, paradoxes in reality vs. what they are told, showcase capitals, and the true existence of North Koreans in the most heinous of circumstances all due to neglect.  So coming to read \\"Nothing To Envy,\\" I expected another matter-of-fact, been there, read that book that re-hashed these themes.  Instead I was truly surprised that Barbara Demick goes beyond her journalistic tendencies to create one of the finest works on North Korea out there.  If I were to recommend a book to someone who has never heard of North Korea, or is curious about it, I would drop this book on them.  Demick's collection of testimonies of several recent refugees from NK, weaving them into a novel-like narrative, while dropping crucial information between each section of dialog and story, is one that she pulls off well and draws out raw emotion from the reader without getting throwing politics in it.  Rather than Demick posing that North Korea is an evil state by giving her opinion like a sauce that she wants you to taste, she let's the testimonies themselves let you decide what to think, and there were times I shuddered at their former life and became thankful for what I do have, and pray for the people of North Korea.<br /><br />What Demick does rightly in the beginning of the book is to lay her cards on the table of how long the book took to research, interview, and compile the information together.  Also, she wisely says that though she has wholesale relationships and stories from the defectors, there are moments where she cannot corroborate them because obviously it is difficult to get information from within North Korea itself.  Then she begins her narrative.<br /><br />She runs through several refugees and their former lives, mostly from the city of Chongjin which is right near the Tumen River on the Chinese border.  Demick describes a young couple, Mi-ran, coming from a low-class of North Koreans because her father was a South Korean, and her boyfriend Jun-sang whose family had come from Japan.  Then she later tells the tale of Mrs. Song, a woman who was a stout believer in the Communist regime to the dotted i.  She thought that nothing could go wrong with their 'eternal' leader Kim Il-sung.  There's Dr. Kim, a petit trained physician with an iron will to do what is right.  Demick describes these and others as normal human beings where they grow up and knew nothing else of their outside world and what they believed what was normal.<br /><br />In between each story and chapter Demick describes in more details/historical analysis of North Korea that gives background to each person's existence, such as the Communist nation's supposed equal status for everyone though they officially classify people based on their loyalty to the state, which has effects even on how much food rations that they could receive.  Or what happened to Mi-ran's father, a former South Korean POW, and what happened to these people of circumstance who were not allowed to go back home.  She describes in great detail the political state structure that creates an environment of mismanagement, like the infamous collective farms or the post-war fall of Communist regimes that led to less support of the Korean regime.  Demick balances a human narrative with a historical background so that you identify with the palate of emotions these people go through while getting the necessary information of understanding their circumstances.<br /><br />The book climaxes with the disastrous famine that occurred around the time of Kim Il-sung's death and towards 1998.  Millions died.  Famine became noticeable when their food rations were cut.  This is the most difficult part to read: there were moments I cringed on how hunger made people turn to beasts or opportunists at the expense of others.  People of Dr. Kim's profession, a noble one, were forced to hunt in the mountains and hills for food and medicine.  Or watching young children die of malnutrition.  At the same time Demick doesn't write a section on blasting Kim Jong-il's regime.  Yet the horror stories of lack of food and the absurdity of the government's \\"let's eat two meals a day\\" paints the worst sin a government will be ultimately judged by:  inaction and neglect.  At this time the author puts in a new view of how North Koreans survived, and it was at times ugly and illegal (at least according to their law), but they managed to survive.  She describes Chongjin's citizens doing what it takes to trade, barter, or steal to put some substance in their bellies, even if it couldn't be digested.  The section about orphans who pick up scraps off the ground was heartbreaking.<br /><br />The final sections of the book describe the the eventual danger and migration of the refugees out from North Korea to China to South Korea.  The dangers of crossing, checkpoints of getting across to another country and their repatriation laws, surviving to the dangers of exploitation, finally to the promise land.  And even there our North Korean friends we read about have more gut-wrenching moments of regret in leaving their homeland and the culture shock that follows from adjusting from state control, \\"you follow me\\" to land of opportunity.<br /><br />Again, one of the best reads on North Korea to date.  I enjoyed reading Demick's writing style and putting together a story, not an informative, static, long newspaper article about North Korea.Its quality is renowned for good use of language, like describing the love story of Jun-sang and Mi-ran that makes you know that under the umbrella of repression and absurd rules, love abounds.  How Demick describes how the darkness of night due to the lack of electricity proves to be the couple's ally in having an innocent love.<br /><br />Sometimes its what we don't have, or what we read others don't have, that makes us appreciate life and moves us to change for something better.

In 10 years I think this fixture has had two CFL's replaced since it's on about 14 hours a day (it's off when I leave for work).  The light is a slightly yellow warm white.<br /><br />The nice thing about this bulb is that it is almost exactly the same form factor as regular lightbulb, so it should be able to fit in any normal fixture.  This was a problem in one of my bathrooms with a very tiny fixture. I think a good pricepoint for this type of light would be about $5-$7.  The current $10 (at the time I'm writing this) is a little bit high, but would be fine if you need it for a fixture that is lit most of the day.<br /><br />It would have a cost savings even against a CFL, and certainly against a regular bulb.  If you are replacing a conventional incandescent bulb, this bulb should have a pay-back time of only about 1400 hours if your local electrical cost is $0.12/kW-hr.  If you replacing a CFL this would have a much longer payback time (several years), since a CFL is already very efficient.  If you factor in the cost of the CFL, and that these last much longer than a fluorescent bulb, the payback gets much better, again on the order of 1000-2000 hours (depending on the life-span and cost of the CFL you are replacing).<br /><br />You should be aware that while these bulb have an extremely long life span (great for those really hard to reach fixtures), they do very gradually get dimmer over the years.

Johnson is now one of my favorite authors right up there with John Sandford.

0143039431|5.0|Grapes of Wrath is historical work of fiction. Covering the migration of tens of thousands of tenant farmers from the mid-west to the West of America. The book is half-fiction and half historical narrative.<br /><br />Amazing and absorbing are two words that initially come to mind when recalling the story. We follow the Joads, a family of tenant farmers, from Oklahoma to California. The mid west has become a dust bowl and the crops are performing badly. The land owners are heavily indebted to the banks who call in their loans. The land owners have to bow as industrialization thunders down on them and tractors and massive earth moving machines plow and turn the land. The tenant farmers are evicted and slowly but surely, reality sets in. They have nothing but each other. All the work is out west they hear. Our story starts as they point the old jalopy west and set off for better days.<br /><br />Steinbeck writes the story from 3rd person perspective and writes using the vernacular one would hear if one were a fly on the wall. Only becomes On'y, something becomes sumptin, every becomes ever', family becomes fambly. After reading for a while, the voice in your head takes on a Southern drawl as you read over the exchanges of the family. Conversation is scant yet, succinct. Articulation is nonexistent but the emotion leaps from the page. It truly is amazing the amount of feeling Steinbeck manages to get across to the reader. You truly are in the wagon, tents, camps, fields with the Joads as their personal Odyssey develops.<br /><br />There are 30 chapters and every other one is a historical perspective that Steinbeck uses to color in the hurdles and forces working against the migrants who were headed West. These folks were treated with contempt, condescension, abuse, left to starve and physically abused by law-enforcement yet, they continued on their way with hope. The hope was eroding from within yet, onwards they went. Tight family units looking out for each other and helping others along the way.<br /><br />The characters all add their \\"something\\" to the story. One of the more interesting ones, for me, was the character Casey. Casey, an ex-preacher, is somewhat like the phone in the old movies. Someone would grab the phone and use it to fill in some parts of the movie. Casey has that role in some places. He's a thinker and we find him thinking and explaining things to the Joads as they come to terms with their predicament.<br /><br />This work is a classic, no two ways about it. It's an American Odyssey during the great depression. Down home, heart of the Nation people uprooted by progress and forced to adapt to a world that didn't want them. Excellent work.<br /><br />6 stars.elping others along the way.<br /><br />The characters all add their \\"something\\" to the story. I stole your purse, set a fat kid on fire, and stitched up some wounds in my leg but I'm totally in shock and can't do anything worthwhile right now.\\"<br /><br />Why does he spend three years in shock being worthless? He also learns to appreciate a warm meal I guess.<br /><br />OK, so after he's done being worthless he goes to the magic school and is a genius. But for some reason he makes enemies with absolutely the worst person to make enemies with. Over time this guy tries to ruin his life several times, eventually even trying to kill him. But our villain is too clever - he never does anything himself, he always hires middle men. Surely at some point Kvothe will actually try and stop this guy right? Maybe spread some rumors that you're going to murder the next person to try such a thing.<br /><br />You know what's really funny? Anytime Kvothe farts and it smells a little off everyone hears about it. It's a big freaking deal. But he gets attacked? Every inn in the city refuses to hire him because someone threatened them? Nobody hears jack about any of that. Nobody in school is sitting around saying, \\"Isn't it weird that nobody wants to hire the best musician in the school?\\" Teachers aren't angry at him for 'conduct unbecoming' when word is out on the streets that he wants to murder the family of people that oppose him.<br /><br />No, everything that happens in the story is so painfully happening just for the sake of the story. At no point did I feel the events transpiring were organic - they always felt incredibly forced. Like the tenth time the character became poor again. Eventually you just kind of stop caring. An event doesn't happen to the character because it's interesting, it happens so that the next event will make sense.<br /><br />That being said - parts of the story are very good, or at least interesting. The few times Kvothe does actually do something it made me very excited and pleased. The writing style is interesting, but often I felt that it also kind of hurts the narrative. Introduce a character - this guy tries to kill me later. Twenty chapters later the guy finally tries to kill him. About time, it was getting annoying just sitting around and waiting for it to happen.<br /><br />The main character is interesting, but he's held up by an uninteresting (and often unimportant) cast of worthless side characters, and even worse than that dragged along from point to point in the story without meaningful or logical direction. Finally he activates his plot device power, and calmed by it he.... tells the truth! Whoa! Amazing, that the truth, easily corroborated, would get him out of most of the trouble he's in. Why couldn't he have done this 4 pages ago? Because he was too buys doing absolutely nothing worthwhile!<br /><br />/EXAMPLE<br /><br />As I mentioned - the plot device power. The character has the ability to calm himself in any situation, allowing him a cool head that he can use to think things through clearly. He uses this power so randomly it doesn't make any sense. It's just there as a way for the author to periodically remind us that, \\"Hey guys, Kvothe is totes awesome!!!\\". When on trial - he uses it. When performing on stage - he uses it. When trying to calm down because his friend is wounded, or a dragon is around (not even directly threatening him) - nothing. He has no real reason to not walk around with this power active 24/7 but for some reason he doesn't.<br /><br />TL:DR - Here's my final word on the subject. The book is interesting, but I have no idea what the purpose of 90% of the story is. Anytime something can happen to advance the story Kvothe can't, won't, or just plain doesn't. He sits around being worthless until the author gives him permission to get off his ass and do something worthwhile, all the while shouting at us in blinding neon letters: KVOTHE IS THE BEST, SMARTEST, GREATEST, SEXIEST GUY EVER!

World War Hulk!<br /><br />Whenever I meet people who snort and turn their noses up when I mention that I'm a grown man who enjoys reading comics, I make the argument that comics are more than simply men in tights flying around the place doing impossible things (though a Superman book here and there is great fun); comics are a more sophisticated, complex, and infinitely artistic medium than to be dismissed outright as serious literature. Superhero comics too have come a long way since the Golden Age and are more than just toy figures fighting one another - just look at the work of Alan Moore, Grant Morrison, Warren Ellis, Garth Ennis, and so on.<br /><br />But books like &#34;World War Hulk&#34; undermine my argument because in this book Hulk batters one hero after another until it becomes representative of the worst excesses the medium offers. It is the stereotype that people who don't read comics think of when you mention comics. Hulks fights Black Bolt and wins; he fights Iron Man and wins; he fights the Fantastic Four and wins; it's just so tedious to read. Hulk smash, blah blah blah, who thought this was a good idea? Is the Hulk literally invulnerable? It seems so. Well that makes for an interesting read, having a main character who can easily defeat any obstacle in his way.<br /><br />Black Bolt is described as being the second most powerful superhero in the world (despite living on the moon) yet Hulk defeats him. How? There's no mention in the book how, it just happens. Thor is noticeably absent in the book - if ever there was a significant threat to Hulk, it's Thor (despite the scene from the Avengers movie showing him as somewhat less tough than Hulk - you know the one I mean).<br /><br />Despite Greg Pak's efforts to make Hulk seem more than the indestructible tough greenie who cuts through Earth's mightiest heroes with incredible ease, Hulk is revealed at the end to be the dummy he's always been portrayed as. He is easily manipulated into going into a rage by someone smarter than he is - I mean, why would Reed Richards et al. try to kill Hulk after the extensive efforts made to put him on a deserted planet where he could finally find peace? Why not destroy Banner when he was out cold when they put him on the spaceship headed to space? But if such glaringly obvious plot holes were pointed out there wouldn't be this book.<br /><br />And having Sentry show up in the final third - why wasn't he the first one to fight Hulk? Because he's agoraphobic. So what set him off to finally get involved? Again, if it was just Sentry fighting Hulk in the first place there wouldn't be a book. It's just so contrived.<br /><br />&#34;World War Hulk&#34; is a dumb comic book that would only appeal to kids who want to see these colourful characters doing nothing more cerebral than punching one another. It's like Royal Rumble with Hulk in the ring and every hero charging at him only to be, drearily, inevitably, rebuffed. The only interesting part was how such an unstoppable force of nature was finally stopped, which I'll admit was at least interesting and probably the only part of the book I thought was clever. But the rest? If all you want from comics is fighting, you'll love this - for everyone else, don't bother.mention in the book how, it just happens. Thor is noticeably absent in the book - if ever there was a significant threat to Hulk, it's Thor (despite the scene from the Avengers movie showing him as somewhat less tough than Hulk - you know the one I mean).<br /><br />Despite Greg Pak's efforts to make Hulk seem more than the indestructible tough greenie who cuts through Earth's mightiest heroes with incredible ease, Hulk is revealed at the end to be the dummy he's always been portrayed as. B004OCCFFI|5.0|For some reason FIDELIO has never been a favorite opera for this listener. True, the great Beethoven is almost beyond criticism, but the work has great moments separated by exceedingly mundane ones.  If the leads in the opera are not strong then the work is hopelessly tedious - for this listener.<br /><br />Then along comes this recording from the Lucerne Festival of 2010 with Claudio Abbado conducting the Lucerne Festival Orchestra in a well synchronized concept that manages to balance all parts of the opera and the entire work comes off making sense - and thrilling sense at that. Analyze the recording and the gleaming gold here is the radiant Florestan of Jonas Kaufmann.  His singing is not only perfectly pitched and technically brilliant, but it also conveys the drama that often is lacking n the voices of other tenors.  Nina Stemme is a credible Leonore and her voice is a fine complement to Kaufmann.  The other roles are adequately sung and the chorus is fine.  But the star here is Kaufmann who seems to increase in stature with every new role and every performance.  He is a treasure to hear.  Grady Harp, August 12

B007UQ5Z1G|5.0|Making their grand debut in 2000 with their phenomenal Hybrid Theory album, Linkin ParkLinkin Park became one of the most famous bands of the 00's. Combining musical genres--such as rock and rap--also made them one of the most controversial bands of all time. Since releasing Hybrid Theory have several albums been released--the likes of which include Meteora, Minutes to Midnight, A Thousand Suns, and finally Living Things<br />Throughout the band's course, however, its sound has gone through some dramatic changes. With Hybrid and Meteora, we experienced a very Nu Metal vibe--that is, with a lot of heavy-metal pieces while also mixing in rap. Minutes to Midnight, to me, seemed to place itself in its own category as the only one of Linkin Park's albums to \\"play it safe.\\" I say this because most of the songs were simple rock with almost no rap at all.<br />Starting with A Thousand Suns and moving onward, Linkin Park has kept their rock-and-rap sound and successfully implemented it into their music--only now with the addition of electronic sounds and pop. Such a pop-like sound would forever split their fan-base.<br />To be completely honest, I barely even care how much the music has changed. Others might argue that no sound of Rob's drums, Brad's guitar, or Chester's screams are heard in any of their new songs. If you believe that--if you want to hear drums, listen to In My Remains; if you want to hear guitar, listen to Burn It Down; if you want to hear screaming, listen to Victimized.<br />I can think of only one reason people wouldn't like Linkin Park's new pop-like style--and that's probably because the only things they can think of with this pop style are the songs from artists like Justin BieberJustin Bieber, Lady GagaLady Gaga, and even Rebecca Black. Once you look past the fact that a lot of our notorious musicians are technically in the same genre as Linkin Park, the music isn't half bad.<br />Only two years after the release of A Thousand Suns does Living Things hit the shelves. With music videos, advertisements, and plenty of lyric videos to comprehend, I think it's safe to say a lot of hype for this album has been weighted onto our shoulders in the past couple of months.<br />On June 26 did this album finally see the light of day in CD stores; such was when I finally bought my own copy of Living Things and popped it into the nearest CD player. In order from the first song played to the last, the list of songs goes: Lost In the Echo, Burn It Down, Lies Greed Misery, I'll Be Gone, Castle of Glass, Victimized, Roads Untravelled, Skin To Bone, Until It Breaks, Tinfoil, and Powerless. The only one of these songs I didn't like was Until It Breaks--but that's probably because too much of it was rap.<br />Lost In the Echo--as the first song of the album--does its job well in introducing us to the rest of Living Things. The song gradually builds up from an electronic beat to rap and finally to the rock-and-roll we've come to love and know with this band. From what I'm able to understand, this song talks about how somebody has been betrayed by a good friend--and are leaving that former friend forever.<br />For the next song, we have In My Remains. This song seems to me like a continuation of Lost In the Echo--as it explains the promises broken in the last song--but in greater detail. Overall, I think this song is just as good as the first.<br />The third song is regarded as the most famous of Living Things' arsenal of songs; it's Burn It Down. Besides being given its own music video, Burn It Down was hosted on the NBA Playoffs shortly after it was first revealed. The song itself has a very unique story to it, as it mainly involves the band and how they've changed. We're building it up to break it back down shows how Linkin Park once built Nu Metal up to its beak--and now they're moving away from it or break(ing) it back down.<br />Lies Greed Misery may seem like a harsh-sounding song during your first hearing--and in a sense, that's what this is. The fourth song of Living Things gives a sense of vengeance toward somebody who has caused great pain.<br />For I'll Be Gone, we get a sense of abandonment from an old friend. That's all I really have to say about this song.<br />Moving on to Castle of Glass, which is possibly the second most popular Living Things song--next to Burn It Down. The lyrics to this song seem to emphasize how miniscule we really are in comparison with the rest of the world. We don't really need to be anything else, according to this song.<br />Now for a song the long-time fans of Linkin Park will definitely enjoy: Victimized. A short 2-minute-long piece, Victimized comes equipped with screams, rap, and everything that made Linkin Park in the first place.<br />The next song--Roads Untraveled--almost made me cry. I say this and I'm not really one to allow a song--or any kind of media, for that matter--to take control over my emotions. Throughout the song's course do we hear an alternation between piano and hard rock--in addition to the switches between Mike's deep voice to Chester's high-pitched sweet vocals. This song seems to show how those who are feeling as if they have no friends will eventually find themselves at peace.<br />Skin to Bone strays away from the personal feel of the other songs--and into a more political sense. Right to left; left to right shows conservatism vs. liberalism. What I think they're trying to say here is how neither of the political sides are right--but only personalities matter.<br />Until It Breaks gives some very strong rap vibes in addition to some of Chester's soft singing sections--and finally it leads up into Brad himself singing. This is the first time we actually hear Linkin Park's guitarist actually sing for us--and it actually turns out to be a bit of a mixed bag. While I admire Brad's voice itself, I can't help but feel like the instrumentation for his part of the song sounded a bit out of place; it sounded like something I'd expect to hear in Pixar's \\"Finding Nemo.\\"<br />Tinfoil is nothing more than an instrumental song--merely a test of the band's abilities. The Hybrid Theory equivalent would be Cure For the Itch.<br />Finally there's Powerless: the conclusion to this musical masterpiece. For those of you who have seen Abraham Lincoln: Vampire Killer, this song should sound familiar. This song talks about how somebody has made a bunch of promises to somebody and how that person has deliberately betrayed them.<br />So overall, I think Linkin Park did a superb job once again. This may very well be their best album since Hybrid Theory--let alone it might be even better than Hybrid Theory. With all that said, I highly recommend this album to anybody who listens to music on a regular basis.'s what this is. B008FLYM50|5.0|I've been OBSESSED with Marina and the Diamonds for the past month. I started off listening to songs from her first album 'The Family Jewels'. It took me a while to get used to her quirkiness and digest her extremely unique voice, but once I did: I was hooked.<br /><br />So, I moved on to 'Electra Heart' Diamandis' second album. (For clarification Marina and the Diamonds is a SOLOIST, NOT a band; the diamonds are you and I, her fans).<br /><br />My immediate reaction was \\"Dang, she sold out.\\" She went from being a brunette to a peroxide-blonde with massive roots, she went from quirky new-wave music, to Britney Spears pop. I initially lost a lot of respect to her. But I gave 'Electra Heart' a chance.<br /><br />My thoughts on this album have changed COMPLETELY. It is REVOLUTIONARY. Seriously, I have not been obsessed with an artist like this since Florence +the Machine. This girl knows what she's doing, this 'Electra Heart' album is meant to be a COMPLETE mockery of pop culture and American society. At face-value it looks and sounds like what you'd expect from a Britney/Madonna wannabe, but once you read into the lyrics and the concepts, you'll realize you couldn't be more wrong.<br /><br />Give her a chance, every person I've introduced her to has fallen in LOVE with her. Even my metal-head friends who listen to screamo have grown fond of her, this is no Katy Perry, this is no ordinary pop. This is revolutionary, catchy, cheeky, genius to say the least. But PLEASE do yourself a favor and listen to 'The Family Jewels' as well, you'll understand this album much better, otherwise Marina will just come off as a dumb bimbo (which was 100% her intention in my opinion). The album overall can be described as Marina's 'Trojan Horse', she is clearly poking fun at the archetypes that pervade our pop culture: the Teen Idol, the Homewrecker, the Beauty Queen, the Housewife, etc. Take a listen, I promise you will love it.<br /><br />1) 'Bubblegum Bitch' 4/5<br />I cringed when I first saw the title for this track, I thought it belonged in Madonna's new album 'MDNA'. It's produced to be radio-friendly, catchy, bitchy as the title suggests. But it reveals a lot about how Marina sees young girls and the culture surrounding them. You will become obsessed after a few listens.<br /><br />2) 'Primadonna' 5/5<br />The catchiest track in the whole album, meant to be so. Produced by Dr. Luke, this tune will stay stuck in your head for WEEKS. 'Primadonna' sounds like something that Katy Perry would put out; it clearly pokes fun at the pop idols of our time with lines like: \\"Get what I want cause I ask for it, not because I'm really that deserving of it.\\" OUCH! This song is also clearly a follow up to Marina's previous hit from her first album, 'Hollywood'.<br /><br />3) 'Lies' 5/5<br />A wonderful ballad, with a dubstep inspired tune. She managed to turn a sad song, into a pop song. Some call it, the ultimate friend-zoned song. Judge for yourself: \\"You're never gonna love me, so what's the use? What's the point in playing, a game you're gonna lose?\\"<br /><br />4) 'Homewrecker' 3/5<br />One of my least favorites, only because it's the ugliest archetype in my opinion. \\"They call me homewrecker! Homewrecker! I break a million hearts just for fun, I don't belong to anyone.\\" I can already see 12 year old girls singing this and posting lyrics on FB enveloped in the vacuous lyrics without realizing Marina's genius sarcasm.<br /><br />5) 'Starring Role' 5/5<br />\\"You know I'd rather walk alone, than play a supporting role...If I can't get the starring role.\\" My favorite song in the album when it comes to lyrics, it's a wonderful and heartfelt song. Perhaps the most genuine show of Marina's ACTUAL personality and not the 'Electra Heart' character.<br /><br />6) 'The State of Dreaming' 3/5<br />\\"Millions of girls float on their one quote\\" referring to Marilyn Monroe's famous \\"If you can't handle me at my worst, then you sure as hell don't deserve to see me at my best.\\" speech. This song is one of the least appealing when it comes to sound, but one of the best when it comes to lyrics.<br /><br />7) 'Power & Control' 5/5<br />Too dark to be a radio-hit, but man is it good. My favorite line \\"Think you're funny? Think you're smart? Think you're gonna break my heart? Yeah, you may be good looking but you're not a piece of art.\\" Watch the video, it's great. Definitely my favorite out of the entire album.<br /><br />8) 'Sex Yeah' 5/5<br />Again, this is one of the songs that initially turned me off along with 'Bubblegum Bitch'. Once I listened to the lyrics (these require a lot less interpretation than the others) it was clear that this monster of a song was not to be compared to the common pop of Katy Perry and Nicki Minaj. \\"If women were religiously recognized sexually, we wouldn't have to feel the need, to show our ass-ets to feel free.\\"<br /><br />9) 'Teen Idle' 5/5<br />Any outsider will be able to relate to this one \\"Wish I'd been a prom queen fighting for the title, instead of being sixteen and burning up a Bible.\\" A wonderfully produced tune with great lyrics.<br /><br />10) 'Valley of the Dolls' 4/5<br />Perhaps the saddest song on this album, this song explores the price of fame in modern society. \\"In the valley of the dolls we sleep, got a hole inside of me, living with identities, that do not belong to me.\\" Social commentary on the production of pop stars, who are really nothing but puppets of the labels. It's refreshing to hear a POP star singing about it.<br /><br />11) 'Hypocrates' 1/5<br />A track that in my opinion could have been omitted from the track list without having a major impact on the rest of the album. It carries essentially the same message as 'Lies' and 'Starring Role'. \\"I know you only want to own me, and that's the kind of love you show me.\\"<br /><br />12) 'How to be a Heartbreaker' 4/5<br />Marina's attempt at breaking through the U.S. market and climb the charts. \\"Rule number one, is that you gotta have fun, but baby when you're done you gotta be the first to run.\\" It sounds like pop, it is pop, not the most mind-opening song on the album. It's catchy, but reveals almost nothing. More of a filler meant to make Marina more famous. Produced by Dr. Luke, expect to hear this on the radio very soon.<br /><br />13) 'Radioactive' 4/5<br />Strangely reminiscent of Rihanna's \\"We Found Love\\", 'Radioactive' was actually produced and RELEASED before the latter. One of the poppiest songs on the album, it's a good listen. Not her best. \\"In the night your heart is full and by the morning empty. But baby I'm the one who left you, you're not the one who left me.\\"<br /><br />14) 'Fear and Loathing' 4/5<br />This song was the first tune to have a video released for it on YouTube, yet it's the last song on the album's tracklist. Huh? It seems like the 'Fear and Loathing' is Marina's true concern for becoming vacuous and shallow like the character she champions through 'Electra Heart'. \\"I've lived a lot of different lives, been different people many times, I've lived my life in bitterness and filled my heart with emptiness.\\" This song is the death of the character 'Electra Heart', her redemption and her fall. Back to the old Marina please!<br /><br />15) 'Buy the Stars' 5/5 [NOT IN U.S. RELEASE]<br />BEAUTIFUL BEAUTIFUL song that is unfortunately not included on the U.S. release, look it up on YouTube. A fine ballad with great raw emotion. Some people say she's talking about the Illuminati's influence on today's pop music, who knows! \\"You bought a star in the sky tonight, because your life is dark, and it needs some light, you named it after me but I'm not yours to keep. Because, you'll never see, that the stars are free.\\"<br /><br />16) 'Lonely Hearts Club' 5/5 [NOT IN U.S. RELEASE]<br />A throwback to 'The Family Jewels' era, this song incorporates quirky beats and sounds that make it one of the least mainstream-sounding songs on the album. One of my favorites. \\"Love will never be forever, feelings are just like the weather. January to December, do you wanna be a member?\\"<br /><br />17) 'Living Dead' 4/5 [NOT IN U.S. RELEASE]<br />This is another non-essential track in the album, so I see why Diamandis decided to not release it to the U.S. crowd and replace it with 'How to be a Heartbreaker'. Though non-essential, it's a shame that U.S. fans won't get this tune on this release. It sounds great, lyrics are ok. \\"I haven't lived life, I haven't lived love, just protect me from the sky above. I lay back in a glittering mist, and I, I think of all the men I could've kissed.\\"tta have fun, but baby when you're done you gotta be the first to run.\\" It sounds like pop, it is pop, not the most mind-opening song on the album. B0011Z78R6|5.0|Less than a half-dozen songs/albums from the 1970's could be truly characterized as bona fide music masterpieces, taking listeners beyond the typical and leaving the mundane and the cliche behind. \\"American Pie\\" by Don McClean and \\"Barracuda\\" by Heart are two which make it on the list. Pink Floyd's \\"Dark Side of the Moon\\" is another. And \\"Hotel California\\", both the title and the album may be the only other. I don't know if I can muster more titles from a decade which saw the rise of idiotic disco music and forever lowered everyone's standards. While most people were dancing to brainless and generic synthesizer music in the late 1970's while wearing open-collared polyester suits, much like today, a few truly dedicated artists were creating music to challenge the listener.<br /><br />The song \\"Hotel California\\", probably the best and most memorable song by the Eagles, has so many levels of meaning and interpretation, it can't be pigeon-holed into being labeled this or that. The singer tells a strange story about when he was driving one night down the \\"dark desert highway\\", either along Highway 5 between San Francisco and Los Angeles, or the other similar highway between Los Angeles and Las Vegas on Highway 15. Although the cover is the Beverly Hills Hotel in the Los Angeles area, the song describes a California-Spanish style hotel, one of which resides in a small truck-stop town about 100 miles southeast of San Francisco. Wherever the song takes place, it's in the Middle of Nowhere, California. Tired from his journey, the storyteller decides to stay at a very strange hotel where there appear to be no lights. He hears the ringing of a bell, like a church. A woman appears at the doorway and leads him in via the light of a candle. There are people whispering and dancing in an open courtyard, some \\"dance to remember\\" while others \\"dance to forget\\". The woman seems like she has some hold over them who appear to be mostly males. At one point he orders wine, and in the most famous line from the song, the \\"Captain\\" says they haven't had the \\"spirit\\" here since 1969. Then there is a reference to a great feast, which seems like a cult-like sacrifice, before our narrator is running for the door. Many interpretations of the song have been offered by different people, and the Eagles are reluctant to spell it out what it all means, which is the true mark of a great piece of musical literature. Some have suggested that the entire experience is a California cult while others have proposed that the Hotel represents the decadence and materialism of California, particularly Hollywood, the Entertainment Industry, the money, and all the trappings that go with it, including those who get imprisoned by its superficial glamour. Even after many listenings over several decades, I always hear something new in this song, or imagine something I hadn't before. Some of the other songs from the album deal with similar themes of entrapment and decadence, such as \\"Life in the Fast Lane\\". A true masterpiece of 1970's rock, not to be missed if you're unfamiliar with it.famous line from the song, the \\"Captain\\" says they haven't had the \\"spirit\\" here since 1969. Then there is a reference to a great feast, which seems like a cult-like sacrifice, before our narrator is running for the door. Many interpretations of the song have been offered by different people, and the Eagles are reluctant to spell it out what it all means, which is the true mark of a great piece of musical literature. Some have suggested that the entire experience is a California cult while others have proposed that the Hotel represents the decadence and materialism of California, particularly Hollywood, the Entertainment Industry, the money, and all the trappings that go with it, including those who get imprisoned by its superficial glamour. Even after many listenings over several decades, I always hear something new in this song, or imagine something I hadn't before. Some of the other songs from the album deal with similar themes of entrapment and decadence, such as \\"Life in the Fast Lane\\". A true masterpiece of 1970's rock, not to be missed if you're unfamiliar with it.

B000VWGYEI|5.0|Those who reviewed this before me referred to this as Elton's first album. Actually, it's not, it's his second. It's a common mistake, though, because this was the first album he released in the US. He released Empty Sky in the UK in 1969, a year before this was released in the US. Empty Sky didn't make it to the US for a couple of more years, so a lot of folks my age think this is his first album since that's the order we heard them in. Anyway, I really love this album. It is more introspective and more complex, both in music and in lyrics, than Empty Sky. These are still some of his best songs, even after all these years. Any Elton John fan who has been limited to his greatest hits type of albums should definitely check this one out. There are real gems here that you won't find on the compilations. In particular, don't miss Sixty Years On or First Episode at Hienton.

People most likely think that: Surely in this day and age this couldn't be taking place in a place like the US.<br /><br />For me it doesn't take a stretch of the imagination to believe.  When I was telling my mother about the book her first comment was: \\"Sounds very European to me.\\"(Meaning reminiscent of the Soviet Union methods in years past). The result was that she broke her neck. Bela Karolyi.)<br /><br />Bottom line is that it really what naysayers think.  It is not so unreasonable to put legislation in to place to protect our youthful athletes. Something like this should be considered for child athletes as well.<br /><br />I do agree with the one reviewer that the writing was a little dry in a few spots and not necessarily written with a skill that one would expect of a published book but I found these things easy to overlook because the story is so enthralling.  Don't get me wrong, this book is NOT poorly written.  It's hard for me to articulate exactly what I mean. But you know going in that writing is not her profession so I found it easy to overlook. Yes, to some degree I could agree with that reviewer but I found it more sweet. B001641ZFS|5.0|I see that there are some Carmignola-Abbado bashers here, and I welcome them: a true democracy needs its dissenting voices. Also, I understand very well how Carmingola-Abbado's streamlined Mozart could shock listeners grown on more traditional approaches, and unable to open their ears and mind to different views. Take the \\"Andante\\" of Sinfonia Concertante. If your expectation of what it should sound like is, say, the 11:51 minutes of Oistrakh in his 1972 recording ([[ASIN:B00000DOJD Mozart: Sinfonia Concertante, KV 364 in E Flat Major, Concertone KV 190 in C Major, Adagio KV 261, Rondo KV 269]]), then no wonder you will be shocked by Carmingola-Abbado's 9 minutes and find that the music is \\"miniaturised\\" - and not just in duration. And I can very well understand how someone accustomed to more orchestral beef and grandeur in the first movement of the same would find the more chamber-like transparency of textures of Abbado \\"miniaturised\\". De Gustibus non est disputandum.<br /><br />But they are wrong, of course, and others don't need to be deterred. It is only a pity for the Carmignola-Abbado bashers that they remain deaf to the great musical beauties offered by these artists.<br /><br />Like: the transparent and lean orchestral textures that never let the basses go muddy or the horns and oboes covered, the wonderful timbral character of the latter, the rhythmic crispness of the strings, all offering muscularity without thickness.<br /><br />Like: the creamy tone of violist Danusha Waskiewicz, and the wonderfully hushed and intimate dialogues with Carmignola in the two cadenzas.<br /><br />Like: the lively and dynamic tempos in the outer movements, exuding an irresistible (well... obviously not to everyone) joy and exuberance.<br /><br />The Carmignola-Abbado bashing also begs the question which approach is truer to Mozart's indications. Sometimes we are so accustomed to a certain, traditional approach, because it is the way we've always heard it and the way it has always been played, that we fail to notice that these are not, by no stretch of the imagination, the tempos written by the composer. And when an interpreter finally plays the piece at what can be reasonably considered as the composer's tempi, we are shocked. It was exactly the same reaction when the Michelangelo frescoes at the Sistine Chapel were removed of centuries of patina (painting conservators call it \\"dirt\\"): the traditionalists were horrified at the loss of the chiaroscuro, the garish colors - only neglecting the fact that this is exactly what Michelangelo had painted.<br /><br />Well, I will grant that Mozart's indication at the top of Sinfonia Concertante's first movement, \\"Allegro maestoso\\", legitimates the more held back and beefy approaches (and even the good traditional approaches don't lack dynamism and explosiveness here); Still I find Abbado's lean and transparent textures, muscularity without weight or thickness, and sense of exuberant joy irresistible. But the Finale is a \\"presto\\", so anything goes as long as it gives the impression of being fast. But I must make a special case for the middle movement: it isn't by mistake or through slapdash carelessness that Mozart wrote \\"Andante\\" here. From the Italian verb \\"andare\\", to go, to move. To generalize, the middle \\"slow\\" movement is a romantic invention of the mid-19th century. Even Beethoven didn't write slow movements in his symphonies. First Symphony? \\"Andante cantabile, con moto\\". Second: \\"Larghetto\\" - not Largo, mind you, and the 7th: \\"Adagietto\\", not \\"Adagio\\". 5th: \\"Andante con moto\\", 6th \\"Andante molto mosso\\", and 8th tops it all: \\"Allegretto scherzando\\". And even when he writes an outright Adagio, as in the 3rd, 4th and 9th, his metronome indications belie the notion that it is \\"slow\\", as we understand it today.<br /><br />Back to Mozart and his Sinfonia Concertante: ever since the early recordings from the 1950s, the tradition (of which the afore-mentioned version of Oistrakh is also a good illustration) has been to take his Andante more like an Adagio or a Largo, turning the music into a solemn, brooding movement in the style of the Masonic Adagio & Fugue. And there is no denying the beauties of that approach, the moving pathos it produces.<br /><br />But, other than the fact that it is certainly truer to Mozart's \\"Andante\\", there are also many beauties in the more flowing approach of Carmignola-Abbado. I find that it elicits a mood of emotional inquietude that makes it more truly \\"pre-romantic\\" than the more overtly romanticizing of the traditional approach through dragging out the tempo and thickening the textures. But certainly, to hear and be sensitive to that mood of inquietude requires to cleanse your ears and mind of any expectations of  Masonic solemnity and pathos.<br /><br />And it is nothing new, you know; In fact, it is striking how close Carmingola-Abbado's timings are to those of Heifetz and Primrose (but of course, Heifetz was often accused of playing everything too fast, by listeners unable to understand his very special artistry and musicianship, [[ASIN:B000003EQ9 Double Concerto]]). And, at his very slightly faster tempo in the Andante, I find that Heifetz brings out even more clearly that mood of inquietude. But while Carmignola-Abbado's first movement is faster than every version I've heard except Heifetz (but I've heard very few on period instruments), look at the Finale:<br /><br />Sammons-Tertis-Harty in 1933 (not what you'd call brainwashed HIPsters, would you?): 5:56 ([[ASIN:B000BTQG8A Father of Modern Viola Playing]])<br /><br />Heifetz-Primrose-Solomon 1956: 5:48<br /><br />Oistrakh-Barshai Moscow 1960: 5:55 (brainwashed HIPsters, truly? [[ASIN:B000U1PU36 David Oistrakh Plays Doubles]])<br /><br />Carmingola-Abbado: 5:51<br /><br />(And for reference: Oistrakh Berlin 1972 6:08, Harnoncourt 6:25, [[ASIN:B008L450NO Mozart: Sinfonia Concertante, Violin Concerto, Kremer, Kashkashian]])<br /><br />So how's that for, as another reviewer has put it, Carmignola and Abbado's Finale disappearing before you know it? If so, at least there is a long tradition for it.<br /><br />OK, that's only for Sinfonia Concertante, the piece I am the most familiar with and which landed me on this entry in the first place. I'll return later, and in due time, to the concertos. But what Carmignola, Waskiewicz and Abbado offer there augurs well for the rest.

B004XXIHS2|4.0|In its first section, A Kiss Before Dying tells an absolutely riveting noir tale of a money-chasing young man who finds out that his meal ticket may have ended up pregnant and has some concerns about getting rid of the child. What could any amoral young gold-chaser do in such circumstances? And what follows is a riveting tale of a young sociopath convinced that murder may be the only way to proceed. It's a great tale, and builds effortlessly to a great climax...but all of that only really contains the first third of the book. What follows from there all comes as a result of that tale's conclusion, as investigations come together, mind games are played, and people do their best to learn about people they think they know. Here's the thing about the rest of A Kiss Before Dying: there's nothing wrong with it at all. It's interesting enough, and has at least one twist that's pretty surprising and beautifully executed. But what the first section does is immerse you in the mind of a killer, and Levin's narration does a beautiful job of creating the mind of someone capable of justifying their own actions in any circumstances, no matter how horrible they may be. It's a fascinating, compelling voice, and with the shift to other narrators in the remaining sections of the book, Levin casts aside his most interesting and intriguing character in favor of some who don't grab the reader nearly as well. That being said, the book is still fascinating enough; it just never attains the heights of that first section, and what follows is a lot harder to become as riveted by.

159514188X|4.0|This book is about a character named Clay Jensen, a Valedictorian candidate and all around 'nice-guy' who finds a package without a return address full of audiotapes. The tapes are from a friend named Hannah Baker who had recently committed suicide, and they tell her story in her own words. Clay is one of 13 people scheduled to receive and listen to the tapes because of his involvement in her death. Many of the people in the list hurt or betrayed Hannah in some way. Clay listens to tape after tape to discover the nature of his scar when he cared for her so much, but too late.<br /><br />Apparently no one taught Hannah how to use a webcam.<br /><br />  I liked the method of story telling and found it very effective. Everything you hear Hannah say is after she had decided to kill herself, and it is a story of heart break and resignation. Everything she says was chosen deliberately to present her case and tell her story. Clay makes the perfect person to react to the story, because he was also present for many of the events described in the book. He offers the perspective of what depression looks like to an outsider, and how the events she presented affected other people. Unlike many of the people in Hannah's life who thought she was looking for attention Clay wanted to help but had no idea how.<br /><br />  The book is called 13 Reasons, but it should be called 13 People. It is about how our actions affect other people and how important it is to listen to and care for one another. It is also about how a suicide affects everyone that person knew and came into contact with, which is something equally important to show in my opinion. I thought it should have gone into more detail about how to get help for those considering suicide, but I am sure there was a website listed in the back or something.<br /><br />Take this subject seriously. There isn't a funny caption here, get help if you need it.<br /><br />  I had the opportunity to listen to this as an audiobook and it is an experience I recommend to anyone considering reading this book. The book follows two characters, Clay Jensen and Hannah Baker, and two different voice actors read their corresponding sections which really gives you a feel for the two voices as distinct entities. As you listen to the short bursts of Clay's reactions to Hannah's story the contrast is glaring and gripping. Though the woman reading for Hannah sounded like she was about 45 for some reason.<br /><br />  The book was depressing, but very emotionally resonant and I think it is an important book for anyone whose life has been affected by depression, self-destructive behavior, or suicide.<br /><br />For the full review and more on books by Stephanie Perkins, John Green, JK Rowling and also Doctor Who and Sherlock, please visit my blog at crazykenna2@blogspot.coman experience I recommend to anyone considering reading this book. She is on medication keeping tumor growth at bay but she carries around an oxygen tank because her lungs 'suck at being lungs'. Ever since her initial diagnosis at 13 and the plateau in her condition after starting this medication Hazel has been living on borrowed time. She got her GED when it appeared that she would never be able to go back to school but started up some community college classes once she was more stable. In order to get Hazel to 'have a life' her parents insisted on Hazel attending a weekly cancer support group, where she meets Isaac and Augustus. Isaac is days away from having his second eye and his last thread of sight removed because of eye cancer and Augustus had his right leg removed above the knee 2 years ago because of bone cancer. Hazel and Augustus have an 'Edward and Bella' stare-down when they first lay eyes on each other, but as they talk and explore each others lives they have a believable relationship. They have a connection based on mutual admiration and not lust. Neither is delusional about the others circumstances, and both make valid observations and mistakes and jokes and reassurances. They awakened something in each other, it was a boy-meets-girl that was more than just a meeting. They were both human, and I loved them.<br /><br />I thought this would be the story of how Hazel died. That is what I was afraid of, because I liked Hazel. She wasn't bitter, or entitled or stuck in perpetual 'What does it all mean?' that most adolescent protagonists are prone to. Hazel made decisions based on how her actions would affect others, she read poetry and marveled at the beauty of nature, she lived with constant pain and struggled with the question does pain have meaning. She could quote TS Elliot and Shakespeare and video games with flawless ease. In the end, this was not the story of how Hazel died, but how Hazel lived. The different writers and the different narrators really bring this novel from good to great; from pleasant to memorable.<br /><br />  I thought at first I liked WG1 much more, because he had a much more agreeable disposition. B0007XTOKC|5.0|Elvis is Back! was the first album Elvis Presley released after his two year stint in the army.  Artistically, the album was a triumph on every level.  There was new depth to his voice; his interpretations were increasingly sophisticated; the group was probably the best studio band in the business; the song selection was imaginative and varied, the technical quality excellent.  Most surprising of all, the new album pointed in no one musical direction.  It wasn't unmistakably related to the sound of his previous recordings.  There was no strong country flavor, and only a hint of gospel in the voices of either Elvis or the Jordanaires.  It was as if Elvis had invented his own brand of music, broken down the barriers of genre and prejudice to express everything he heard in all the kinds of music he loved.  This two cd set collects the original album, the non-LP singles recorded at the same sessions, plus tons of alternate takes.  This is the ultimate version of one of Elvis Presley's best albums.

1401937268|4.0|At first I wasn't so sure about getting this deck; I had purchased a tarot deck some years ago by Lucy Cavendish, but it didn't contain the Minor Arcana. I stopped using them, not for the lack of the Minor Arcana, but because I wasn't so sure tarot was for me and I didn't really know what I was doing. Fast forward over seven years later, and I decided that I at least wanted to get a user friendly deck that really spoke to my spirt. It wasn't that I didn't have a connection with Lucy's deck, it's just that I didn't know if it was an oracle deck, or a tarot deck and I felt that I wasn't getting the most out of them, which led to misusage and eventually bad energy.<br /><br />Shopping around for a deck was very frightening at first; I was just going to go back to the Women's Oracle deck, because I felt that I wasn't finding a deck that spoke to me on a spiritual level. While looking for some books in Barnes-n-Noble, I came across Angel Tarot cards. They didn't wow me, but I still picked up the box, looked at the art work, put it down and moved on. I decided to do some research on Doreen Virtue, and her Angel cards. What got my wheels turning was a weekly reading video I saw on YouTube. It featured the ArchAngel Michael deck, and everything about the cards seemed pleasant and uplifting. After that I couldn't stop thinking about the different decks. Over the next few days I prayed and mediated, finally I went out and brought the Romance Angels deck as well as the Angel Tarot cards deck.<br /><br />After reading several reviews on Amazon about how large and unpleasant the deck was to shuffle I was instantly leery of the cards, but I decided its always better to experience such things for myself. Once I got home I didn't get to know them right away because I wanted to check out the other deck. Later on that evening I opened the Angel tarot deck and the first thing I noticed was how uncomfortable they felt in my little hands. I was needless to say turned off, I was also turned off by the cards themselves. It was as if they were \\"silent.\\" They didn't speak to me and I felt no connection at all; I kept thinking to myself \\"Great, I just wasted time and money on a deck that I probably wont use...\\" BOY WAS I WRONG!<br /><br />I don't know why I was initially not connected to these cards; but as soon as I cleared the energy and prayed over them, they started yakking away! If anyone does know or have some insight as to why this may have been, please let me know. Learning and something I love to do, and I'd love to get some feedback from a seasoned reader. I started off by doing a three cards spread, and I was so impressed at how accurate each card I pulled spoke to my current situation. I got so impressed with them that I decided to really test the waters and do a Celtic card spread, which is intimidating enough for a beginner such as myself, because I've NEVER had any clarity with reading this particular spread. Well, not this time; the cards were so easy to read, and each position was so on target that it scared the crap out of me! It was as if my closest friend was allowing me to step back and take a look at myself from a broader perspective.<br /><br />Now for the deck itself: The illustration is magnificent, I mean it. Each card is colorful and eye catching, not to mention the detail is exquisite. The Major Arcana is represented by a particular ArchAngel and gives you a little insight as to why that particular Angel represents the card it's illustrated on, via the guidebook. I liked the concept of replacing the Death, Devil, and Tower cards with Life experience, Release, and Ego. It's not that the Death card actually means Death, or the Devil card actually means monster; however we all know that many people, due to many factors take these words to be literal representations. It can be hard for a beginner to restructure his or her mindset to think otherwise. At least with this deck, they can start off with more positive words, that represent the same meanings as the original names.<br /><br />Other names have been changed and I can honestly understand why; again, I'm assuming that she didn't want people to focus on the literal meaning of the original word. A fool is not necessarily a literal idiot, however beginners can have trouble making the necessary adjustments. By calling the card The Dreamer, you do get a sense that this person is eager, untainted, unexperienced, green and full of promise. The Judgement card is has been renamed Renewal; I'm assuming because the word judgement may come across as too harsh, or confusing, or again maybe she just wanted to keep with a positive, more uplifting flow. The only card that I was kind of iffy about the replacement of the name was the Heirophant and Temperance for Unity and Balance, respectively. It could be that when people think of Heirophant they think of one religious denomination instead of a general spiritual aspect.<br />Although, changing the names for a more positive outlook is wonderful it can also have it's draw backs. It can make the learner lazy, and it may keep them from learning how to adjust their mode of thinking, which is what in my opinion makes the Tarot card so unique; it helps us think differently about any given situation or area of life. It can also turn off a seasoned user who is merely looking for a new deck, and sees it as a dumbing down, or a passive aggressive approach to the message the cards can be trying to give.<br /><br />I found the guidebook to be an amazing tool; the despriptions were clear and full, and an additional meaning is offered as well as a description of a type of person the card could represent if needed. This actually helped me read the cards better; offering a wider aspect of why the card may have come up in the first place. I also loved the explanation of why she changed the order of the Justice (8 card) and Strength (11th card). She also explains the importance of NOT dismissing a card that may pop outof the deck while shuffling, which to my surprise always seemed to get to the root of the question I was asking.<br /><br />She used some ArchAngels to represent multiple Major Arcana cards which at first seemed redundant, but every Angel has more than one duty, so it makes since that he/she would appear on more than one card. I also liked the element usage for the Minor Arcana; Air, Earth, Fire and Water. As an earth sign, I felt a particular connection with the imagery displayed on them, but I have a few gripes. First, if this is an Angel deck, why are unicorns, elves, dragons, fairies and mermaids involved? I consider them to be mythical beings, not actual entities like the Angels themselves. IMHO, it takes away from the power and realness of the Angels; almost as if they're being lumped in as just another group of fairytale creatures. I know there are many people who believe in these beings, and no disrespect to anyone who does, but for someone like me who sees them as a cute concept, but not a real force within our universe, it comes across as kind of campy and Tolkien like.<br /><br /> So if there is someone out there who is seeking a way to reach out to the Angels, or someone who may want to get to understand how Angels can assist us, but doesn't relate to the concept of mermaids or, someone like myself who doesn't believe they exist, it could be a turn off. However, I say, don't let the fact that YOU DON'T BELIVE IN THEM, STOP YOU FROM TRYING TO CONNECT WITH THE BEINGS YOU DO BELIEVE IN. My belief is that the beings merely represent the element they appear in. Ex. Mermaids for water, Dragons for fire, Unicorns for air, etc... I also kept in mind that just because I DON'T BELIEVE, it doesn't mean someone else doesn't, and out of respect for all belief and spiritual systems I understand why she put Angels and Elves in the same deck, this deck is for EVERYONE. Again I still think it waters down the concept of \\"Angels\\".<br /><br />Another thing I'm disappointedwith would be the size and comfort of the cards; they're huge, hard to shuffle, and it takes a lot of work to break them in. I  decided that when shuffling the easiest way for someone like myself would be to split the deck in two; shuffle one stack good and proper, do the same to the other stack, bring them back together and do the \\"kiddie shuffle,\\" then cut the deck. It worked and the message was just as accurate, if not more, than it would have been if the cards were a user friendly size.<br />The one thing I do enjoy is the fact that the fairies, elves, mermaids are all represented by multiple races of people. This made me, being a woman of color, feel more comfortable with the fact that she at least understands that many people from all backgrounds will be using this deck, and would enjoy seeing all types of races, and cultural backgrounds represented. Which is why I also understand why the Fairies and Dragons are also used, it's that Universal appeal concept which is again great. It also shows that spirituality isn't just a representation of one being, race, color or creed. However, I noticed that none of the ArchAngels are represented in this way; I guess that would have been too risqu, Kidding!!! :)<br /><br />All and all I really do love this deck, and I find it an amazing study tool and a great choice to actually learn how to properly utilize a tarot deck, without confusing oneself, becoming frustrated, and or feeling overwhelmed. Although the seasoned tarot user may be a little annoyed by the deck's \\"failure to keep it true,\\" a newbie, and or a non traditionalist who isn't caught up in the \\"if it's not broke, don't fix it,\\" ideology will really like using this deck once they've come up with a method of shuffling. Be aware however, once you tap into the angels, and make that connection, don't be surprised if you channel one or two,they pay you a visit keeping you up all night chatting. You'll know them when they arrive; their presence will be strong and soothing, yet awesome and scary (in a good way), at the same time. Simple solution: Thank them for keeping a constant visual over you, paying you a visit, and kindly let them know that you are heading off to bed; after two hours of just trying to tune them out I politely asked them to ssshhhh, and bid them goodnight so I could get some sleep. LOL<br />God Bless, and enjoy!

0465068367|5.0|Some history writers forget that there are readers at the other end of the book they're writing, and believe that reciting gruesome facts is enough to tell a story. Iris Chang understood that she needed to retell the story of the Rape of Nanking so that modern readers would care and be outraged. She offers us heroes too, unlikely ones, especially John Rabe, the German Nazi who had lived most of his adult life in China, and who stood up heroically for the victims of the Nanking genocide. The book offers a stark truth about heroes; sometimes, they end up really getting screwed in the end. A man can be the most ordinary person who engages in one of the most heroic acts of the 20th century, and still end up dying in obscurity, poverty, all of one's heroic acts forgotten by the rest of the world.<br /><br />It's doubly tragic that Iris Chang herself became another victim of the ghosts of Nanking, committing suicide after the book was published, leaving behind a husband and an infant son. After reading this book, which I've had to walk away from several times because of the intensity of emotion and anger it causes for me, I can't even begin to imagine how difficult it must have been for Chang to interview survivors, document their stories, look at diaries, documents and personal collections of photos of the Rape of Nanking.<br /><br />I finished this book learning a few important things besides the details of the history of the genocide in Nanking. If you ever have the opportunity to witness history first-hand, take a lot of pictures, and make sure you get that film (in our modern world, it would of course be digital images) safely into the hands of someone outside the country as soon as possible. This is what some foreign observers did, because they knew that without photographic evidence, nobody in the outside world would ever believe their stories.

The WHAT to eat is merely a consequence of that. After thorough but understandable and easy to read lessons in physiology, biology, and psychology, they carry the reader right into their flagship program, the Whole30. Thirty days of allowing your body and mind and relationship with food to heal. The key piece of this book, however, is not even this Whole30 program.  The key which makes this book worth the investment is their instruction for AFTER your Whole30 - how to use the Whole30 to conduct an experiment of one and see exactly how different foods affect you.  You gain valuable knowledge about yourself and how you engage with food, so that going forward you can make choices that are smart and informed for your best interest. B001F0WXY0|4.0|Review of the novel itself:<br /><br />I had planned to give this e-book to my son as part of a long-running series of introductions to the classics of science fiction that began with Heinlein juveniles when he was young. I read Dune (on paper of course) in about 1980, and thoroughly enjoyed it at the time; I've picked it up and re-read bits of it from time to time over the years--I still find I enjoy the writing and the world Herbert created, and can open this book to almost any page and just delight in re-reading for a few minutes. Of course, some of the themes seem to fit better in the 1960's than 2010's (e,g., drugs to access special mental powers), and some seem dated even for 40 years ago (e.g., gender roles and re-creation of a feudal social structure), but all in all I enjoy this work much more than other books that re-create an essentially feudal social structure in a high-tech future, and some bits I find more plausible than most modern SF (like the explanation of why there is no significant computer technology, which is a bit of a leap. But it is consistent with human nature and, unlike a lot of SF, not actively inconsistent with computing technology, information theory, etc.). I never really enjoyed the many other books set in the universe of Dune, though I did read the two that were available about 1980. But the 535 pages of paper that were the original \\"Dune\\"--those were great.<br /><br />Review of the eBook and art of publishing:<br /><br />Returning to the subject of the e-book edition, I was initially put off by the \\"Kindle Version Review\\"; but (as that reviewer pointed out) Amazon does not seem to attach reviews to specific editions, so I couldn't really tell if this review still applied to the current ebook. I got the free sample, and found almost none of the errors that others have reported; I spent the money to buy the full thing, and so far (35%, up to page 184 of 474) I've noticed very few problems ... I had Kindle search for one of egregioustypos pointed out by another reader (\\"stulsuit\\", I think it was) and did not find it. All in all, the errors are few and far between (see below).<br /><br />In case anyone cares, the errors I've found by page 184 are several joined words and two other minor issues:<br />* Page 4, italics are missing (this was the same in the GoogleBooks edition, suggesting that the publisher gives the same file to all ebook providers)<br />* Page 42, two cases of joined words: arevaluable and dealingwith<br />* Page 71, \\"iong\\" should be \\"long\\" (an interesting error, as noted below)<br />* Page 106, hypen should be an emdash (\\"heavily armed-slow-pellet stunners\\" should be \\"heavily armed--slow-pellet stunners\\") [Rats ... in the \\"preview your review\\", I can see that my unicode emdash has been turned into a pair of hyphens. Hopefully you get the idea]<br />* Page 176, joined words \\"nosign\\".<br /><br />I actually checked some of these (and a few other things) against my old paperback and the printed 40th anniversary edition (I had to visit a bricks & mortar bookstore); the above are differences between the print and eBook (e.g. the mis-print of \\"Harkonne\\" for \\"Harkonnen\\" on Page 16 is there in both paper and eBook versions).<br /><br />The pattern of errors suggests an interesting theory about the creation of printed books and eBooks: on page 71 of the printed 40th anniversary edition, the word \\"long\\" has a slight gap in the ink of the first letter, making it look almost like an \\"i\\" (though to a human eye, clearly still an \\"l\\" with a gap). It looks very much as if the publisher (a) prints the books from physical type rather than e.g. a phototypesetter (I'm guessing this is cheaper; possibly the type set by a machine, based on a computer file), and then (b) *scans* the printed text to produce the eBook (which seems, to me, odd almost to the point of inexplicability).<br /><br />Overall Summary: I'm glad I got this, and look forward to giving it to my son.
0812993292|4.0|There's nothing surprising in this quintessentially English love child of \\"The Sense of an Ending\\" and \\"Forrest Gump\\", but it is well executed, avoids the worst excesses of tweeness and makes you care about the central figures of Harold and his long estranged wife, Maureen. Harold's impromptu quest to walk from his home in the southwest of England to the extreme northeast seaside town where a woman he once knew is dying, seems at first to be a bit contrived and metaphorical, but Fry grounds his travels in simple experiences that pull his trip back from the brink of absurdity and into the realm of plausibility. As Harold's path meanders northwards his recollections and growth follow a similarly sinuous progress. The way to Berwick-on-Tweed is not as straightforward as it would seem from looking at a map, much as dealing with being human is also filled with detours and digressions. The ending is well earned and quite satisfying and I can see why this first novel was long listed for the Man Booker Prize despite it's conventionality.<br /><br />Fry's distant and clean narrative voice, which echoes the restraint of self that has been Harold's defining characteristic in life, also serves to save this modern take on a pilgrim's progress from the bathos that overwhelmed \\"Forrest Gump\\". This is a gentle book, and although you know that Harold will be redeemed, how he works his way to admitting to himself what is behind the steel doors of his memory is worth reading despite the fact that careful readers will know from the beginning much, if not all, of what he is withholding.
