B007R21JJK|5.0|Very nice board, very heavy and solid.<br /><br />Built a system with this a couple of months ago, utilizing an Intel 3750K processor, 2x4GB G.Skill Ripjaws X memory, and a Sapphire Radeon 7850 PCI 3.0 video card hard-coded to GEN3 in the BIOS, along with two system boot drives in a RAID 1 configuration.<br /><br />It's been rock-solid and stable, running Intel Turbo-Boost (set to take the processor up as high as 4.0Ghz from 3.4Ghz), with the memory running in Turbo mode with the XMP Profile enabled, and with the latest \\"official\\" BIOS (F11).<br /><br />There was only one issue, which almost caused me to return the board to Amazon.  When I first powered up the system (before installing the graphics card), I had the monitor plugged into the on-board video output.  There was no video.  The BIOS would boot up, and stop with what looked like diagnostic code \\"Ab\\" on the LED (\\"Ab\\" is \\"Detect SCSI Devices\\"), with the monitor saying \\"No Signal\\".  I couldn't get into the BIOS.<br /><br />At first, I assumed that the board was dead, and I'd have to return it.  Then, some searching on the web revealed that \\"Ab\\" is often a mis-reading of \\"A6\\".  \\"A6\\" is \\"BIOS is waiting for a command from the keyboard\\".<br /><br />A little experimenting revealed that if I powered up the system with the monitor disconnected, got to \\"A6\\", and THEN connected the monitor, I would get video and could work in the BIOS.<br /><br />This was inconvenient, but once I got my graphics card installed and connected the monitor to the graphics card, everything worked as expected.  I considered knocking a star off for this, but decided to treat it as a minor startup transient.

1419387243|3.0|The Perks of Being a Wallflower has been out for a long time and I finally took the time to read, er listen to it. The audiobook was on sale. Over the years Perks, has received a lot of great praise. It's been a New York Times bestseller, it was a cult hit, lines from the novel have been quoted around the world, and it's even been made into a movie. For all the hype, I have to admit I was expecting something more. It's not a bad story but whatever the message was, it was lost on me.<br /><br />To be up front, this book features difficult teen social situations, sexuality, sexual abuse, drugs and alcohol, and mental health issues. If you are not in the mood to go in depth about these subjects, quit while you are ahead. The story is told through the journal entries of Charlie, a 15 year old socially awkward but brilliantly smart high school student. Charlie starts off the story by taking about the suicide of his best friend. The death is hard on Charlie, and to make things worse, he enters high school without friends. Luckily, Charlie finds some upper class men who befriend him and seem to get who he is. Through these friends Charlie is introduced to some new experiences (cue the drugs, alcohol, and sex). As he goes through these experiences, Charlie reveals more of his past and the reader learns more about what happened to Charlie to make him the way he is.<br /><br />There were two factors that made me not enjoy this book, the narrator is the first and the sheer number of social issues featured is the second.<br /><br />The Narration<br /><br />I strongly recommend reading this book instead of listening to it as I don't feel the narrator did a very good job. The narrator is clearly not 15 years old, and to imitate a teenager he talks in a funny high pitched voice. Charlie's character has a strong sense of innocence about him and the narrator makes Charlie seem dumb instead. After awhile, the narrator's voice wore on me and I regretted choosing the audiobook over a paperback.<br /><br />The Social Issues/Drama<br /><br />There are a lot of social issues in this book and definitely overwhelming at times. Reading a book with this many problems may be fulfilling to some but I'm not among them. I can see how a book about these topics can be refreshing for some but for me it was tiring. I feel shallow saying it but in all honesty there were just too many social issues in this book. Way too much. On top of it, it's told from the perspective of 15 year old who really doesn't entirely understand what's going on. Also, these issues are never really resolved. Time passes and things get better for some of them. In other cases, the problem bubbles up later. With no resolution, the story felt like it lacked plot. The author may have been going for this, since in a way it's like real life, but at the end things felt unfinished.<br /><br />Conclusion<br /><br />Overall, I wanted to like this book, I really did. There were so many great things said about it but I didn't really see it. I think the author is talented and he wrote some poetic prose. His story also held my interest but in the end I was disappointed. I don't think I can tell you what the perks of being a wallflower are either.aperback.<br /><br />The Social Issues/Drama<br /><br />There are a lot of social issues in this book and definitely overwhelming at times. It's wonderful escapist fare, and I'm grateful. He seemed to have an excellent grasp of his characters and their employment situations, and the way pharmaceutical companies work, enough to be credible and make a fast compelling plotline that moved along very well. Should be made into a movie.<br /><br />I had a few technical issues with it:<br /><br />1)I didn't like the way David was introduced to the other lawyers, Finley & Figg. It wasn't explained more than once that he saw the address on the side of the bus, and that's why he landed there from the taxi. Getting that drunk was too extreme and not credible to me. Other reviewers have commented on this.<br /><br />2)What was most difficult to believe was where David suddenly launched into all the research he had done. This research could have been introduced slowly instead of being dumped at the actual trial. At first, he seemed to be winging it at the trial, and then he showed too many specifics not to have really organized and planned his research by memory. I thought he was supposedly busy at the office doing other work, not just gearing up for the trial although some preparation is mentioned, just not that much.<br /><br />3)The women were portrayed like cartoon characters, especially the two women in the courtroom, admittedly an unnatural situation. It just seemed too extreme from a masculine point of view, a bit too chauvinistic and condescending to the women, except for the fashionable female courtroom star and surprisingly, a sex symbol. (The latter part was not believed by this reader.) The women, except for Rochelle, were not well-described. I would have liked to read more about David's wife, who must have been very intelligent, but didn't get enough credit. It wasn't even mentioned that she missed the best part of the trial where David performed at the end.<br /><br />4) What bothered me the absolute most was where David took out his anger on the blogger with impunity. Bloggers (like me) are unpaid, and we believe we are adding something to the world, and don't deserve to be punched out in revenge for any reason (let alone killed as bloggers are in Mexico). Blogging happens to be serious and useful writing. Lofty Harvard-educated David wasn't even punished! That bothered and scared me, actually, that a lawyer got away with it. And how many people actually were influenced by that blog in the story except David? It's unlikely that the mythical blog had many readers, and to take out aggression on a blogger doesn't set a good example for the rule of law in international societies. Worse, it didn't make sense; the blogger actually inspired David to work harder and say something in the defense after reading a bit of criticism, right? I think the action against the blogger in the story was mean, gratuitous, and unhelpful to bloggers in general, a sphere of writing that Grisham appears not to want to help, oddly enough. Maybe the author should take up a bit of blogging to see what it's like, to see how it is for bloggers, as the more wealthy author Danielle Steel has, for example. (But Dan Brown doesn't blog either last time I checked, so he is not the only author who isn't blogging).<br /><br />5)Makes the point that David doesn't carry a gun, but doesn't go into the virtue of gun control enough, and makes a gun seem like a good weapon to have. Bothered and annoyed me a bit that the opportunity to push for weapon-banning didn't happen. Harvard-educated David should have been been more rewarded for not carrying a gun somehow, instead of seeming snobby, weird, nonconforming, unrealistic or unprepared for not having a gun. But sadly, the gun-toters Finley & Figg seemed to be admired and rewarded with saving their own lives by using guns in the story. David's role could have expanded to include and show reasons why guns don'twork in all situations. Now that takes imagination. Otherwise, the wild west rules, and guns appear as the easy way out. I know editors like guns as solutions, but really, why wasn't David shown to be a hero for not carrying a gun instead of seeming wimpy and ineffective in that way?<br /><br />6) David would have been a better example of a nice lawyer by not getting quite as drunk at the beginning, and not punching out the blogger, and still have appeared realistic and credible. He could have had other faults that were a bit less extreme, less violent and alarming to women, like not wanting to wash his clothes or clean the house or something else, like being afraid to fly to describe why he hardly goes anywhere.<br /><br />7)If the reader knows that it isn't anything like a John Galsworthy book as far as having basic command of descriptions of nature, then it's fine. It's such an urban novel. Could have had more country scenes, apart from the warehouse scene with the toy company officials.<br /><br />8)The variety of points of view jumping all around made me go back and check for POV at times.<br /><br />Overall,from the point of view of exciting action, it was a wonderful story. It set up a fine story and delivered. I admired it and enjoyed it. It deserves an enormous readership, and I recommend reading it. Would make a great movie as well. Makes me want to go back and re-read his other novels again.

0439023521|5.0|Katniss Everdeen volunteers to be District 12's female \\"tribute\\" to the annual Hunger Games because she can't bear to let her little sister, 12-year-old Prim, accept that lottery assigned role. Katniss knows it's a death sentence, because each district must send two tributes to the Games - one boy and one girl - and only one can survive. The tributes will be trained, placed in an arena with unknown conditions (anything from desert to arctic), and left there until they've killed each other off. That is the ongoing punishment the Capitol visits on its once rebellious districts, and as far as Katniss knows no one has ever challenged the Games and their rules in 75 years. Winning means a lifetime of ease for the winner, and a year of Parcels for every citizen of that young person's district. Since people commonly starve to death in District 12, those parcels mean survival. And at first, Katniss doesn't care if she has to kill the male tribute from District 12 along with everyone else who enters the arena. Peeta, the local baker's son, was kind to her once when it made all the difference; and Katniss's reaction to that is hated of indebtedness. She only hopes that her well-honed hunting skills, learned from her dead father and practiced through years of illegally taking game to feed her mother and Prim, will be enough to carry her to victory. Unlikely though that seems, it is all she has...and then things get worse, as she learns that Peeta loves her and she begins to want him to survive, too.<br /><br />This young adult novel is, like so many good ones in that class, entirely readable for adults as well. It's scary enough so I'm glad I could not encounter it while I was an YA reader myself, quite a few years before its publication. Its first person, present tense viewpoint is one I don't usually enjoy, but in this case that viewpoint works because it gives immediacy to Katniss and her situation. One crisis follows another - at times the story moves almost too fast, as both Katniss and the reader struggle to keep up with what the Gamekeepers as well as her fellow contestants are throwing at her. The one thing I find hard to believe is Peeta's sincere and completely self-sacrificing love for Katniss, which doesn't change no matter how she behaves. He seems a bit too saintly, in that respect at least, to stay credible.<br /><br />A real roller coaster ride of an adventure that has a point, as a good YA novel always does. In this case the point (in my estimation) is that people don't know what they are capable of until survival, their own, hangs in the balance. I agree with the reviewer who says that one must accept the author's premise in order to suspend disbelief, and I can understand why that might not work for everyone. But it did work for me.<br /><br />--Reviewed by Nina M. We're 4 books into the planned 7 series and there is only the faintest links at this point between the three. Martin introduces new characters at will, making it nearly impossible to keep track of who is actually important, who is involved in what, and who is allied with who. While I enjoy plot twists, Martin puts in so many, with such frequency and seemlys so haphazardly that I'm coming to believe that there really is no plot. B0071XO8RA|5.0|Hugh Howey's self-plublished series of 5 interlocked stories about life in a destroyed and desolate future are as entertaining and heart-pounding as you could want.  A couple of years ago there was a lot of buzz and fervor for Justin Cronin's THE PASSAGE, and reading WOOL with anticipation and readerly joy made me wonder how does buzz start?  Because WOOL deserves that buzz.  It is highly readable and full of shocking twists and turns.  And it takes place in a fully imagined and realized world that is rife with detail and intrigue, and it was, up to and including its last page, able to constantly surprise me, which is no mean feat.  Do I take slight issue with a couple of narrative shortcuts to get story out through characters who seem connected only to get that information out?  Sure.  But that issue is very slight.  As speculative fiction goes, WOOL is at the top of the heap - and I would suggest that label is just too limiting for this very enjoyable story (for the 5 books really are just one big tale). 1400052181|5.0|\\"Henrietta Lacks\\" is one of the best books I've read in a long time. It tells the story of the famous \\"HeLa\\" cells, removed from an African-American women with virulent cervical cancer, that proved to be so hardy that they were the first to be reproduced in a lab. But this is most decidedly not a scientific treatise. This is the story of a black family in the 1950's in Baltimore on the eve of desegration. It's the story of a medical establishment that rode roughshod over them in the name of science. It's a story about race, culture and ethics.<br /><br />Skloot became fascinated with the \\"HeLa\\" cells in high school and spent years researching their story. What she tells here, though, is really the story of Henrietta's family as they struggle to reconcile the loss of a mother with the crazy tales they are told about what their mother's cells mean to science. Along the way, we read of numerous examples of medical experiments conducted without patients' knowledge or understanding, much less consent, exposing many of them to serious harm. What's more, these patients were most often the most disadvantaged members of society--poor, black, institutionalized or infirm. The details Skloot provides are truly shocking. At the end of the book Skloot provides a well-written, very clear summary of where the issues of medical experimentation stand today, and it's not what I thought at all. Beware!<br /><br />Rebecca Skloot has mastered the art of writing non-fiction that reads like a novel. The only thing I've read that's as good as this in this genre is \\"Warmth of other Suns.\\" [[ASIN:0679763880 The Warmth of Other Suns: The Epic Story of America's Great Migration]] One other recent example of this genre that did not come up to the mark was \\"In the Garden of Beasts.\\"  [[ASIN:030740885X In the Garden of Beasts: Love, Terror, and an American Family in Hitler's Berlin]]. It's a rare skill, and Skloot has it.

1582701709|1.0|In Plato's \\"Republic\\" Socrates urges caution on the nature of sophists - itinerant and deceptive frauds posing as legitimate educators - and implores his listeners to be skeptical and rational in the face of hucksterism. Philosophy by its truest definition can best be summarized as a system of shared knowledge. Rhonda Byrne's mendacious \\"The Secret\\" works on one basic contingent: your wallet. It is prefaced with the most erroneous claim that the greatest individuals throughout history (Plato, Newton, Darwin, etc) \\"secretly\\" - there's that word again - embodied this philosophy, which the unfortunate and hapless reader is coaxed in to believing over its terse 170 pages. It is the kind of unsustainable philosophy endemic of a time when pseudo-scientists would have us believe that the cosmos is aligned with human beings and alchemy is chemistry. Despite how overwhelmingly preposterous and commerce-minded The Secret is, it has nevertheless flourished, because of its readership's perennial curiosity with spirituality and their \\"place\\" in the universe. There is nothing wrong with asking the big questions on life and who we are, but there is something grotesque when you consider that these basic human inquiries are reduced to televangelist style fundraising for a very elite few. One can find all the probing moral and spiritual questions in the works of people like Kant, Darwin, Hume, Locke, and Epicurus. Those individuals devoted their entire lives to the big questions of existentialism, metaphysics, nature, and science; this book is not so high-minded. Even if you remove the monetary motives, the core philosophy is still utterly ludicrous. Essentially it's this: if you sincerely want something, and \\"put it out there\\", then you'll get it, but if your desires don't manifest, then through no fault of the method it is your own. This is classical circular reasoning. At the very point of its conception it has its own built-in fail safe clause, which guarantees the philosophy, but cannot guarantee the person using it. Equally confusing as its ideology is its financial success. And therein lies, excuse the pun, \\"The Secret\\". Because it is so terse in philosophical specificity, but so broad in its aims, it seeks to bring its readership into the manifold through suspension of critical thinking and recruitment of logical fallacies. It is breathtakingly irresponsible and stupid. This book is not the result of a considered philosophy, but a flavorless by-product of committee thinking. The Emperor is without clothes.

B005K0HDGE|5.0|Growing up, Stephen King was easily one of my favorite authors. Many of his novels created indelible impressions in my mind. (I still have a distrust of clowns thanks to It, and just thinking about The Stand makes me cough.) But my fondness for King has wavered a bit over the last 15 or so years, as I started discovering that as his novels grew in size, his ability to close out a story (from often exceptional ideas) suffered somewhat. Needless to say, I put a great deal of thought into deciding whether or not to read his latest opus, the nearly 900-page 11/22/63, but after flying through it in less than a week, I can unequivocally say this novel should be ranked among some of King's best work.<br /><br />Jake Epping is an English teacher in Lisbon Falls, Maine, who teaches GED English classes as a way to make extra money. In response to an assignment in which he asked his students to talk about a life-changing incident, he learns that Harry Dunning, a handicapped janitor, was injured by his father during a violent rampage on Halloween night in 1958, the night he killed Harry's mother and three siblings with a hammer. This discovery deeply affects Jake, and when his friend, local diner owner Al, shows him a hidden time portal in the diner that transports you back to September 1958, Jake jumps at the chance to go back in time and prevent this massacre. No matter how long you're \\"back,\\" when you return to the present you've only been gone for two minutes, and if you ever go back through the portal again, everything that happened on your last visit resets. Once Al realizes he has a kindred spirit in Jake, he enlists him in the ultimate heroic mission--stopping Lee Harvey Oswald from assassinating John F. Kennedy. But time and history do not enjoy being diverted from their plans...<br /><br />There have been thousands of books written about time travel and the idea of righting past wrongs, but in Stephen King's tremendously capable hands, this concept seems fresh and unique. And when a book of nearly 900 pages reads like a 300-pager, you know you're in the hands of a master. Is some of the plot predictable? Sure, but it's still utterly compelling. Don't be put off by the heft of the book or its subject matter--at its core, this is a story about love, history, and trying to do the right thing, even though it may have larger ramifications. Truly a fantastic book.

B00338QENI|5.0|Henrietta Lacks was a poor Southern woman and mother of five children who died in 1951 as the result of a remarkably aggressive form of cervical cancer. While undergoing radiation treatment, doctors at Johns Hopkins took samples of her cells without her consent, to better study the cancer. These cells, known as HeLa, became an important scientific and medical tool--they were the first \\"immortal\\" human cells grown in culture, and they are still alive today. HeLa cells were vital for developing the polio vaccine; uncovered secrets of cancer, viruses, and the atom bomb's effects; helped lead to important advances like in vitro fertilization, cloning, and gene mapping; and have been bought and sold by the billions. But very little was known about the woman from whom these cells came, and her family was unaware of Henrietta's contributions to science for many, many years.<br /><br />The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks tells Henrietta's story and chronicles the struggles of her family, most of whom never had the opportunity to even finish high school, to understand what cells were taken from her and what scientists were doing with them. Rebecca Skloot also gives a great deal of background into scientific research, particularly where cell and tissue study is concerned, and the challenges both the scientific and medical ethics communities face relative to \\"donations\\" of cells taken from patients. But in the end, this is the story of Henrietta's family and the emotions that the discovery of her role in science uncovers.<br /><br />While the subject of this book is very scientific, much of it reads like a novel. It is a fantastic story--sometimes shocking, sometimes heartbreaking--but Skloot gives depth not only to Henrietta and her family, but the researchers who both studied and questioned the HeLa cells through the years. When this book was released last year to tremendous acclaim, I didn't think it would interest me much, but a friend recently recommended it, and I'm glad I was motivated to pick it up. Don't be put off by the subject matter; in the end, this will pique your curiosity and tug at your heart. And you'll definitely look with a different eye at the next medical procedure you have!

1400052181|3.0|Hi guys,<br /><br />I had to read this book as a part of a summer reading assignment for high school and thought I would share my opinion. I intend for this review to be a sort of \\"heads-up\\" for other teenagers thinking about reading this book because, to me, it read like it was geared towards an older audience. That said, I realize that it has a spot on the New York Times bestseller list and a great deal of positive reviews here on Amazon. I'm not trying to make the point that it shouldn't have these things -- in fact, I have a feeling that I would have rated it higher if I were 20 years older right now. Setting this aside, I just want to give my perspective of this book as a teen reader, so here it goes:<br /><br />I was excited when I first got this book because I'd heard about HeLa cells on an NPR program called Radiolab. Flipping through the accolades, it seemed like the book would be just as engaging as the Radiolab segment. For me, it wasn't. On one hand, the book was very informative; you could really sense the years of interviewing, researching, and other hard work Rebecca Skloot put into writing the book. On the other hand, it was all the details about this process that became the most annoying for me to read. Going back to the point that this book is both a bestseller and positively reviewed here on Amazon leads me to speculate that I'm simply not old enough to appreciate the extensive details about the research process.<br /><br />To help explain what I mean, I'll turn to my personal experiences with writing. I've written a number of research papers over the years and similar to Skloot (though obviously on a much, much smaller scale) each one of these papers took months of research, bus trips to libraries, analyzing primary documents, talking to teachers, and even e-mailing a few random college professors for advice (on a whim). All of this was accompanied with the thorough note taking, draft writing, and proofreading that a lengthy research paper entails. What I ended up with for all of them was a final product that presented a theory and backed it with carefully selected evidence. While reading this book, I felt that Skloot was also trying to present and defend a theory: that it was unethical for a Johns Hopkins hospital doctor to take cervical cancer tissue samples from a woman (Henrietta Lacks) without her consent and subsequently neglect to acknowledge her contribution to some of the greatest medical advances of the century. However, along with this theory were extensive biographical elements regarding how Skloot went about gathering information for the book. I think that, in addition to the age barrier I mentioned earlier, these details weren't particularly interesting or engaging for me because there was no clear pattern to how she switched between Henrietta's narrative and her own. In that sense, I would call the book disorganized. To me it seemed like she was trying too hard to blend it all together, which just made the book harder to read. To be clear, this was not a page-turner for me. I had to take breaks regularly while reading it and scribble a lot of notes in the margins to keep track of what was going on. A comparable scenario would be trying to read one of my papers with all of the details about my research mixed in. It just wouldn't work.<br /><br />I think Skloot's approach was too distracting for me partly because I'm a teen reader who grew up in the \\"Google\\" generation. Because I'm used to having answers instantly, I think I would have enjoyed the book a little more if it were primarily plot-driven. Instead, Skloot spent a lot of time discussing how she came to learn about a given fact from Henrietta's life, the evolution of assorted bioethics issues over time (i.e. consent, profit, morals, racism, etc.), and the specifics of the daily (and honestly pretty mundane) struggles of Henrietta's descendants' lives. While all of these \\"side\\" subjects are certainly relevant, it was easy to get lost intrying to filter/sort all of the information she presented. Heck, I feel like Skloot had researched them all thoroughly enough to write a different book about each one!<br /><br />Regardless of my opinions about the book's format or the intended audience, I believe the book succeeded in its ultimate goal of eternalizing the story of the woman behind the cells. It was very comprehensive and humanizing -- you could get a clear sense of what Henrietta was like. My ultimate message to teen readers is that this book is a long and involved read (not the type of book you'd take to the beach), so don't go in with the same expectations you would in reading the plot-driven Harry Potter series, a moving biography, or an informative Wikipedia article; this book attempts to combine all three qualities with mixed results.

1400069289|5.0|Many of us pick up popular neuroscience books with a question at the back of our minds:  How can the content in this book help me?  If this is your main reason for picking up the book, the first part on habits and individuals will be extremely interesting, but you might decide to skip the rest of the book about habits in business and society.  If you did, however, you would miss some fantastic material, also very relevant to each reader.  There are some interesting theories about the elusive success of AA, some original, somewhat frightening, research about Target and marketing, and some truly inspirational stories about change in society.<br /><br />In the latter sections of the book we learn that an important factor in creating lasting change is belonging to a group that supports that change.  As Americans who tend to individualism, this is an important lesson for us.  Another, though never explicitly implied, lesson is that lasting social progress is possible.  Quite good news for those of us jaded and depressed by modern life.<br /><br />Personally, I found this book relevatory about myself and my family.  We suffered with a very cluttered house for years.  When we moved one mile away, I threw out bags of stuff, but the clutter never seemed to dissipate.  When we re-located to the States, we flew over and rented a house, but all of our things traveled by container ship, taking a leisurely six weeks.  When it finally arrived we pulled out a few things we genuinely missed and, puzzled by our own change of hearts, left the rest in the garage.  Reading this book, I finally understood why.  Expecting our things at any time, we hardly bought anything new.  Over six weeks we had generated the habit of living in spacious, tidy rooms.  An expensive way to replace a habit, but by golly, it worked.

B007ZBWJXI|3.0|Looking at all the reviews here could intimidate a person into not writing a review of \\"XXX\\". The viciousness of the 'fans' here is amazing. So, let's get this out of the way first: For me Asia was always Downes, Howe, Palmer and Wetton. Always. I'm not saying anything about Payne because I never bought and listened to Payne-era Asia. Except for 1992's \\"Aqua\\" and I think 1994's \\"Aria\\", both of which were sub-par, and it had nothing to do with Payne.<br /><br />I also liked \\"Alpha\\" and \\"Astra\\". Sorry, but I think both of them are good albums alongside \\"Asia\\". Even Mandy Meyer on \\"Astra\\" does a stand up job in the absence of Howe.<br /><br />So what's my problem with reunited Asia? Well, it's not Wetton's vocals--I think they are still strong if a little weathered by age. It's not Howe's playing, though I do agree it's not as aggressive and it feels lower in the mix. Then I get to thinking about Palmer's drumming, and I think it's not as interesting or adventurous or as aggressive as it once was. But for me, the main culprit is Downes. I don't like his choice in synthesizers on these last three Asia albums. His choice of keyboards and synth sounds are, well, cheesy. It seemed to me that he was more careful about keys on the first three Asia albums from the 80's. I keep thinking Downes is out of touch with the latest technologies.<br /><br />So, no guitars the equivalent of Howe's \\"Heat Of The Moment\\" licks. No drums like \\"Heat Of The Moment\\". No synths like the very distinctive \\"Only Time Will Tell\\" (or even \\"Video Kill The Radio Star\\") or \\"The Smile Has Left Your Eyes\\" or \\"Rock And Roll Dream\\".<br /><br />And, the more I think about it, the more I think the band is truly missing it's secret weapon--producer Mike Stone. He is deceased now, but he really knew how to structure the band's music into something great. He knew how to capture the essence of this band and polish up real nice. That's been missing from 2008's \\"Phoenix\\", 2010's \\"Omega\\" and now \\"XXX\\".<br /><br />To me, \\"XXX\\" is no different from \\"Phoenix\\" or \\"Omega\\", in fact you could put all three into a blender and hit random select and they would all sound like they came from the same sessions.<br /><br />The key tracks are \\"Tomorrow The World\\", \\"Bury Me In Willow\\", \\"Face On The Bridge\\" and...and...and that's really it. The rest of the songs are slick and faceless. I don't like the religious bent of songs like \\"No Religion\\" or \\"Judas\\". I don't like the midnight mix of \\"I Know How You Feel\\". So the album just kind of sits there--neither terrible nor great.<br /><br />I expect more from these guys. I've been saying that for three albums now. And don't give me the pat response of, \\"Well you can just listen to the first three albums over and over and over then.\\" Get real, you know this band could be doing so much more than this. The production values are weak--they need a real producer who might understand what sound is needed--someone like Jack Douglas, or Tom Werman, or Ted Templeman, or Robert John \\"Mutt\\" Lange, or Bob Rock, or Alan Moulder, or Flood, or Butch Vig. Somebody, any one of those would do.<br /><br />Okay, fire away, click that negative vote button. \\"XXX\\" is just mediocre work from a solid band. Admit it to yourself before you click.<br /><br />I'm hoping they choose to make another album, but with an establish producer. Reign in Downes please. Somebody tell him he is out of touch on the keys. And could somebody please kick Palmer and wake him up from his slumber--we need more energetic and complex drum patterns please. And will someone unshackle Howe? And, of course, let's give Wetton something interesting and passionate to sing about, not some cliche biblical figure like \\"Judas\\" or some castrated doe-eyed gelding singing about \\"Faithful\\". Let's have some bite to the words next time.<br /><br />Here's how \\"XXX\\" compares to Asia's previous works:<br /><br />1982 Asia: Five Stars<br />1983 Alpha: Five Stars<br />1985 Astra: Four Stars<br />2008 Phoenix: Three and a Half Stars<br />2010 Omega: Three and a Half Stars<br />2012 XXX: Three and a Half Starsnd a Half Stars<br />2010 Omega: Three and a Half Stars<br />2012 XXX: Three and a Half Stars

B002VWNIAY|4.0|This review is for the 2009 DVD release.  I won't touch on the film itself.  If you've seen it, you know how great it is; if you haven't seen it, it's more than worth the cost of the DVD.<br /><br />The only noticeable issues with the transfer are small things that I would attribute to the master being decades old.  Nothing that takes away from the film at all, and still much better than some of the imperfections you see watching a movie in the theaters near the end of its run.<br /><br />The only hangup I have is with the aspect ratio which is why this release gets 4 stars instead of 5.  Casablanca was shot in 1.375:1 (sometimes abbreviated to 1.37:1).  'Widescreen' in movies didn't come around until about 10 years later.  This release is in 1.33:1, which is your standard 4:3 non-widescreen TV ratio.  Because of the cropping you lose about 2-3% of the image (depending on how it was cropped).  Granted, you'd be hard-pressed to notice a big difference without watching them side by side, but I would think they could have done a little better with a new release.  The Blu-ray release is advertised to be in the full 1.37:1 ratio.<br /><br />With the exception of the cropping, it's a good copy and I'm mostly happy with it.

B00425S1H8|5.0|Purchased to interface with lab equipment from Tektronix, Agilent, and SRS. I've gotten it to work with Tek from a Linux laptop (works without any driver installation -- just plug it in!... and I wrote my own software to control the instrument and grab data from it). I'll be trying with Windows soon and the other manufacturers' equipment, but I expect no issues with either.<br /><br />Note that this is a male DB-9 with screw receptacles. Don't expect it to interface with adapters that also have screw receptacles, even if they're female adapters. In the past I've hack-sawed the screw receptacles off so I could plug this directly into a female DB-9 DCE device that also has screw receptacles, but I don't think that's the best solution. Just get appropriate adapters or cabling, it's just a few dollars each and then you can screw things together to better guarantee connections are maintained.<br /><br />The Tek and HP/Agilent equipment I've worked with have male DB-9 connectors (with screw receptacles) and act as DTE devices. Therefore you'll need a null modem female DB-9 to female DB-9 cable there. The SRS equipment has female DB-25 connectors (with screw receptacles) and act as DCE devices. So you need a female DB-9 to male DB-25, but it should not be a null modem cable.

B008645YEE|4.0|Christopher Nolan's Batman trilogy ends with \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" and Hans Zimmer returns to bring the musical journey to a satisfactory conclusion as well with a very capable but somber score.  The reference to the Dark Knight is apt especially for this final chapter, but then the story of Bruce Wayne and Batman has always been shrouded in shadows and inner turmoil and Zimmer has labored, along with James Newton Howard, to capture these shadows musically.  This time out Zimmer is without the actual contributions of Howard, but we assume not without his influence and support. And this time the tone is clearly the darkest of the three film scores as left to Zimmer.  This is a dynamic effort with orchestra, synthesizer and chorus, one that clearly matches the dark story on the screen.  It embraces the solitary and troubled soul of Wayne and the chaotic challenge of Bane (and others?) to Gotham City and its people.  There is little semblance of a romantic theme here, indeed even the Batman motif is itself always there but in a shadowy form.  In fact there is not a great deal of new music in terms of themes but there are motifs that emerge with Bane, and those combined with earlier themes are drawn together for a grand climax.  This is a score that works so well on the screen, however many will find it too dark, and at times overly chaotic, on its own.  But it does work and comes to a finale that is a proper and fitting conclusion to the Dark Knight trilogy.  This is quite unlike Zimmer's jaunty music for Sherlock Holmes, and even darker than the scores for the two earlier Batman entries, but it is bold in that capacity and a solid effort.  Well produced and adequately packaged by Water Tower Music.

Each of the BD versions has at least one (often more) Bonus Features, in HiDef, which were not on the DVD versions.<br /><br />Next, bad news.  Film I had three BFs on the DVD version which were not included on the BD version.  Also the DVD version was the Extended Cut and the BD was not, nor were there Deleted Scenes on the BD version.  Similar issue for film II -- Director's Cut on DVD but not on BD and no Deleted Scenes (though the BD did have all the BFs from the DVD).<br /><br />More good news. All of the DVD versions had a Text Commentary track from the Okudas.  This is a version of the film with popup boxes from time to time with tidbits of info.  I like these text commentaries over audio commentaries because you can watch and listen to the film while still getting extra info.  With audio commentaries, you mostly can't hear the film dialog so you either listen to the film with no commentary or you listen to commentary.  All of the DVD versions had this text commentary but none of the BD versions did (that I could find).<br /><br />So, to sum up -- the BD set has more Bonus Features than the DVD set, but is missing a few BFs and some scenes that were available on the DVD set.  The BD set is also missing the text commentaries fromthe Okudas.<br /><br />And so my end rating is four stars.  The film quality itself is good enough to allow for five stars, so any adjustments I make are based on features.  Although I get some extras here not on DVD, I'm missing others, and my OCD won't let me give five stars for not completely superseding the previous format.

009193768X|5.0|With its' distinctive \\"eau de nil\\" (water of the nile) blue-green cover and subtle gold lettering, this little book not only traces the history of taking tea, but also the history of Fortum & Mason.  There are wonderful recipes like plum and almond tartlets and exquisitely photographed pages with beautiful china plates and tea cups.<br /><br />One interesting fact for Americans is that after the Boston Tea Party in 1773, Fortum & Mason continued to ship its tea to North America to both \\"Loyalists and Republicans alike\\".  Even a war could not stop the Americans love of tea as tea symbolized wealth and status.<br /><br />Fortnum & Mason has long been connected to the Royal Family and in March 2012, Queen Elizabeth II, the Dutchess of Cornwall (Camilla), and the Dutchess of Cambridge (Kate Middleton), spent the day at Fortnums sampling treats and inspecting the Fortnum tins of food being sent to the troops abroad.  The Queen even wore a matching eau de nil suit.  All of this adds to the ambiance of Fortum & Mason as a luxury store which still holds royal warrants as far back as Queen Anne and remains today as the \\"grocers, provisions merchants and tea merchants\\" to the Royal household. This book would make a marvelous gift for anyone interested in the history of tea, recipes for delicious tea treats and in the history of a famous store that has been in London's Piccadilly since 1707.

B004SIP9B0|5.0|Just as every American knows where they were on 9/11, every Brit knows what they were doing when the news came in that Princess Diana had been killed in a car accident in Paris. Heck, I was a thirteen year old New Zealander and I still remember running home from my grandmother's house (having seen the news report on television) to tell my parents what had occurred. It was a shocking, confusing, upsetting time, and for anyone who remembers the media circus in the wake of the princess's death, \\"The Queen\\" provides a fascinating look at what might have been going on among those who were affected by it most.<br /><br />In 1997 the British public elected in a new Prime Minister by a landslide majority; he was Tony Blair - young, popular, and with republican sympathies. In many ways he was the polar opposite of Queen Elizabeth II in nearly every respect, and their initial relationship (at least as it's depicted here) was rather cool. Yet not long after Blair officially accepts the position, a much greater news story sweeps the country: Princess Diana has been killed in a car accident. Yet her death is just the background for the central dynamic of the film: the tentative alliance that forms between Blair and the Queen, and the questions that arise surrounding the nature of grief, and how it should be displayed.<br /><br />As the public begin to grieve their loss, they grow increasingly frustrated by the silence emitting from the royal family. Though the Queen insists that she must attend to her grandchildren and maintain dignity by not indulging the media, the public clamors for a more shared display of regret. They want flags flying at half-mast without understanding the protocol behind it. They want televised eulogies from every minister, celebrity and royal family member in existence. They demand that the Queen \\"lead the people in grief\\", whatever that means. They gather en-masse at the gates of Buckingham Palace to light candles and lay flowers for a woman they'd never even met.<br /><br />The Queen is baffled by it all, stating: \\"we do things quietly in this country, and with dignity,\\" but in doing so, seriously misreads the mood of her people. Blair on the other hand, steps up to the plate, coins the term \\"the people's princess\\" in his eulogy and increases his own popularity. Witnessing the growing antipathy toward the Queen, he tries to advise her to address her people, only to be met with stubbornness and pride. Something has to give, and Blair knows that the longer it takes for the Queen to make a public statement, the more it will seem as though she's simply bowing to public pressure.<br /><br />At the time it all happened, I really had no idea of the furor surrounding the actions (or inactions) of the royal family until much later after the event. Only by watching this film did I really begin to grasp the circumstances of that time. One suspects that most of the frenzy was whipped up by the media who needed a scapegoat for - if not Diana's death - than at least the aftermath, but the royals' inability to judge the needs/wants of the people was a serious oversight on their behalf. The film shows remarkable restraint in providing an impartial look at the events of those sad weeks, presenting the backstage events as they (may have) occurred, and thus allowing the audience to reach their own conclusions as to what should have happened regarding the public and the palace.<br /><br />The film concentrates mainly on the relationship between Tony Blair and Queen Elizabeth, specifically Blair's changing attitude toward the Queen; initially finding all the pomp and circumstance rather silly, getting immensely frustrated with the lack of cooperation from the palace, and finally delivering a final impassioned speech in the Queen's defense that is the closest the film gets to taking a side in the whole affair. (And as the Queen astutely points out afterwards, the reason Blair is so sympathetic to her plight is that he knows all too well that the public could turn on him just as easily - as indeed they did ten years later in the wake of the war on Iraq).<br /><br />Helen Mirren won the Oscar that year for her portrayal of Queen Elizabeth, and it's not difficult to see why. Capturing the mannerisms and speech patterns of the Queen without making it look as though she's trying too hard, Mirren presents a woman who is both strong and fragile, determined yet easily-led, wise in certain circumstances but foolish in others, witty and yet detached - in other words, a fully formed woman struggling with a PR challenge that she can't fully understand. There's a lovely moment in which the Queen is by herself out in the countryside (her car just having broken down) and she's facing away from the camera, allowing herself to cry over the ordeal for the first time. We're not sure why - is it for Diana? For the way she's been treated by the public? But whatever the reason, she eventually composes herself, and her face resettles into its dour \\"game face\\". Right in front of your eyes she goes from a woman to a Queen.<br /><br />Michael Sheen (who has played Tony Blair twice before in [[ASIN:B0010X73Z6 The Deal]] and [[ASIN:B003N9ASF2 The Special Relationship]]) is clearly quite at home playing this particular character, and James Cromwell provides quite a scathing rendition of the boorish, thick-headed Prince Philip, whose main contribution to events is to give his wife consistently bad advice. Alex Jennings is a nervy but surprisingly sympathetic Prince Charles and Sylvia Syms gets some of the best lines as the Queen Mother (\\"nobody tells me anything,\\" she snipes at one point). Wisely, Princes William and Harry are out-of-focus for the entirety of the film, appearing only from behind or from a distance. It's a respectful portrayal of the two individuals who were undoubtedly hurt the most by this entire ordeal, and director Steven Frears is mindful of their privacy.<br /><br />Speaking of whom, the real fascination with this film is how director Steven Frears and writer Peter Morgan manage to make it so darn interesting. Considering all it really involves is various people watching news reports, discussing things in conference rooms, attending press conferences and uncertainly moving from one place to the next, it's quite remarkable that the whole thing remains so rivetingly fast-paced (ironically, their shared DVD commentary is deathly boring). Replicating speeches that were said and outfits that were worn, as well as using stock footage and newsreels that aired at the time, all provide a sense of authenticity and immediacy. Little bits of trivia are peppered throughout (the Queen cannot vote; she was a mechanic in wartime) and though some segments can feel a bit heavy-handed (the symbolism surrounding that stag!) it remains a film that's fundamentally about topics not many people can resist: the lives that go on behind closed doors, the one-sided relationship that exist between leaders and followers, the frenzy of gossip and the media, the manipulative tactics of public relations, and of course - those royals.<br /><br />As a companion piece, try following this up with the identically named docu-drama [[ASIN:B00342ANSA The Queen]], which explores more of the challenges that Queen Elizabeth has faced during her reign.ll that the public could turn on him just as easily - as indeed they did ten years later in the wake of the war on Iraq).<br /><br />Helen Mirren won the Oscar that year for her portrayal of Queen Elizabeth, and it's not difficult to see why. 0061692042|5.0|Count me as one that is a little surprised by the negative reaction of some to Ford's latest novel. I'm chalking this up to the possibility that the Bascombe series caters to a different part of the Ford persona, and that those readers that come from Independence Day to Canada are disappointed by the \\"other\\" Richard Ford. The easiest transition would have been from the first novel, A Piece of My Heart, or the brilliant short stories of Rock Springs.<br /><br />I will also disagree with those who believe that Frank Bascombe was a more hopeful figure than Dell Parsons. They are both quintessential Ford people. Dell experiences more severe headwinds which are the plot elements that make this a significant work for galvanizing the author's feelings concerning the importance of maintaining a pragmatic balance in solving the puzzle of life. This is the central theme of Canada. You could certainly argue that the real Dell may not have fared as well as the fictional one, but this is a novel, which is always a work of the imagination created for the purpose of sharing ideas about human interaction and circumstance.<br /><br />I've always found Ford easy to read. The cadence of his prose appeals to me in a  personal way with characters that have depth and nobility. He has always displayed a certain callous edge to what are otherwise compassionate protagonists, meaning that highly charged emotional episodes can be accurately observed as significant events followed by a philosophical interlude in which the character regains perspective, becomes pragmatic, forms a strategy for survival. If understated, Ford expects the reader to infer the pain, rejection, loss, and misery with the understanding that the goal is to overcome, focus on the situation at hand, and try to move on. He uses Berner as the example of what happens to really good people every day when they lose hope and Dell as the example of one who could put the past and it's harrowing tale of abandonment behind. The factthat the siblings are twins indicates that Ford clearly understands the thin line that separates these perspectives.<br /><br />I really cannot say more without revealing plot, but from a prose point of view, Richard Ford is at the top of his game. He still has a lot to say, and I'm listening.

This would last a minute or so, and then things would go back to normal. After some research, this seemed to be a long known problem on the mac. One of the websites recommended using the iMic, as it was reported not to have this issue. So far, for me, this has been true (maybe around 10 hours online so far, so would assume it would have happened by now).<br /><br />The sound produced by this device is very clean, and I have no complaints with the quality. I believe it's stereo only, not simulated surround. But it is sufficient for my needs.<br /><br />I have no idea why the iMic works, and the previous USB sound devices I tried didn't. But that indeed seems to be the case. So if you were having the same problems I was having, you might want to give this device a try.

I then discovered that it does two things very well: Retrieving photos (even RAW format) and videos via USB directly from my Canon EOS 7d to my iPad, and transferring videos and photos from my iPhone 4 to my iPad. It allows me to select the photos and videos that I want to transfer and my iPad displays them (even the RAW Canon images) in all their glory. It does the same for images and videos on my Sony Cyber-shot HX30V when a micro-USB cable is substituted for the mini-USB cable, and does so without the compression loss of Sony's Play Memories Wi-Fi app. The second connector that is supposed to read SD cards is worthless since the iOS upgrade that limited power to external devices. Bottom line is that this kit only works with self-powered devices, but is so handy to use with my EOS 7D, Sony HX30V and iPhone that I'm glad I did not give up on it. I'm told that it works with most other Canon and Nikon cameras, but you need to read more reviews to find out if it works with yours.

038572179X|4.0|Readers who've objected to this book -- and those who do tend to object rather strenuously -- have focused mostly on its ending, which they have found, at the very least, unsatisfying, and at worst a betrayal of their good faith in a sort of literary partnership with the author. You know, \\"You, kind sir, write the book, and we will be carried away by your brilliance, insight and gift of language -- but you'd better not go too far.\\"<br /><br />McEwan, in \\"Atonement,\\" went rather too far for readers who expect linearity and those who get especially attached to their characters. Without revealing what the author does at the end, let's just say that it involves a truly omniscient twist that some regarded as nothing less than cruelty.<br /><br />But guess what? Life can be cruel. And this author knows that, and for this reader's money, has crafted a stunning work of fiction that reveals much about our human predicament. For one thing, if McEwan is correct, it does turn out that perception really is more important, or at least AS important, as reality. A child's perception, in \\"Atonement,\\" wreaks havoc in several lives. In my own life, I can point to several people whose perceptions damaged me in the extreme. In a couple of those cases, the person in question realized the mistake and took pains to correct it, mitigating or reversing the damage they'd done. In a couple others, the person involved maintained -- against all evidence to the contrary -- their rightness, even though they were brutally wrong. I have lost at least one relationship and one job because of a single person's perceptions, and in both cases, those perceptions had nothing to do with reality.<br /><br />That's why I have defended and even championed this book over the years, recommending it many times to friends and colleagues. In addition to calling attention to the vagaries of perception, it just happens to be beautifully written. And yes, the characters are so wonderfully and completely drawn that when their \\"true\\" fates are revealed, we feel the loss as gut-wrenching, almost intolerable.<br /><br />But I suspect that was the author's intent.<br /><br />If you value true honesty in your fiction choices, and if you are a reader inclined to take things more philosophically than strictly emotionally, I think you will find great value in this book.n their \\"true\\" fates are revealed, we feel the loss as gut-wrenching, almost intolerable.<br /><br />But I suspect that was the author's intent.<br /><br />If you value true honesty in your fiction choices, and if you are a reader inclined to take things more philosophically than strictly emotionally, I think you will find great value in this book.

Now comes the band's 4th album.<br /><br />\\"Bloom\\" (10 tracks + 1 hidden track; 60 min.) starts off with an entrancing \\"Myth\\", and that song sets the tone (and tempo) for the entire album pretty much. When listning to the next track \\"Wild\\", which is probably the most readily accessible tarck on here, I couldn't help but think how majestic and large that song sounds, and frankly most of the album. It feels like singer-lyricist finally has the necessary confidence to show ambitions that were not readily apparent before this album. It's hard to pinpoint highlights as such, as the album truly plays as a unit, rather than a hap-hazard collection of songs. Please note that the supposedly 16 min. closing track \\"Irene\\" is really a 5 min. song, followed by 8 min. of silence and then giving way to a 3 min. hidden track, which is a pretty weak song actually, so for me the album really concludes at the end of \\"Irene\\".<br /><br />In all, the slow but steady rise of Beach House continues with this album. \\"Bloom\\" sounds more polished, more ambitious, and grander than any of the previous albums. I had the good fortune of cathing Beach House in concert at the 2010 Coachella festival, touring in support of \\"Teen Dream\\", and that set was one of THE highlights of the festival for me. I can only imagine that they sound even better now. Meanwhile,\\"Bloom\\" is HIGHLY RECOMMENDED!

A world with two moons. A cult that turned violent. A leader who abuses young girls, including his daughter. A novel about chrysalises and \\"Little People.\\" A teenage girl with a mysterious past. The mood and feel are similar to those of The Wind-Up Bird Chronicle and Kafka on the Shore.<br /><br />However, the dialogues undermined the storytelling. When one character echoes the others, we could attribute it to the quirks of that individual, in other words, characterization. But when every character echoes others, then the dialogue annoys and detracts from the story.<br /><br />Murakami tells the reader that Tengo is a math genius but he didn't convince me. A math genius is unlikely to teach cram school. But the main issue is that Murakami didn't show that his protagonist is a math genius. B0051BNRTO|4.0|I love Nick Hornby. His novels aren't serious and deep, and he obviously isn't trying to win a brand-name literary prize or reach the pinnacle of the literary pecking order. He's just very good at the basic job of a novelist: creating characters who are real that his readers will care about and involving them in a realistic plot that reaches a satisfactory conclusion. Here his main character is Annie, a 39-year-old woman living in a nowhere seaside town in the north of England who wonders how she ended up spending the last 15 years of her life with Duncan. Duncan is a dweeb who teaches at the local college and obsessively chronicles on a website the life and lyrics of Tucker Crowe, an American rocker who bailed on his career at the height of his success promoting a album entitled Juliet 22 years earlier. The story opens with Annie and Duncan on a Crowe pilgrimage in America: to the home where he was born in Bozeman, MT; to the house in Berkeley where Crowe's Juliet lived; to the Minneapolis bar bathroom where he decided to call it quits on music. They return to England to find that a promoter has mailed them the demo of a new Crowe album called Juliet Naked, and after their different takes on the album -- Annie's reasoned thumbs down and Duncan's delirious thumbs up -- go live on the Crowe website, havoc ensues. Duncan falls into a relationship with a new colleague and Annie falls into an email flirtation with Crowe. If a woman had written this book, it would be marketed as a contemporary romance, and that's what it is. Hornby leaves out the cute stuff that's required in a rom-com, and the finally-getting-together scene happens off the page, but he delivers the obligatory happily-ever-after.
